Oaths by Allah, His Names, and Attributes: Custom as the Basis
Explaining valid oath expressions and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which expressions constitute valid oaths according to the Hanafi school, explain why custom is the basis, and evaluate the opposing views of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, any expression customarily understood as an oath is binding — even without the word 'billah'.

When does a person's statement become a legally binding oath? This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the Hanafi position: oaths are built on custom and usage. Any utterance that people customarily treat as an oath — whether by Allah's names, attributes, or even the words 'aqsamu' (I swear) — is valid, regardless of intention or mental state. The text also contrasts this with the stricter requirements of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who demand explicit mention of Allah's name or specific intent.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the valid oath expressions
List the expressions the text explicitly says constitute valid oaths: by Allah, ar-Rahman, ar-Rahim, by attributes like might and majesty, and the verbs 'aqsamu', 'ahlifu', 'ashhadu' even without 'billah'.
Also phrases like 'la'amru Allah' and 'aymu Allah'.
2
2. Understand the Hanafi response to the sin objection
The text anticipates an objection: why is expiation required for an insane or coerced person if there is no sin? The Hanafi reply distinguishes between the evidence of sin (breaking the oath) and actual sin. The ruling is based on the evidence.
Compare to the analogy of istibra' (waiting period for slave women).
3
3. Analyze the proof from Quran and Sunnah
The hadith 'Whoever swears an oath, let him swear by Allah or be silent' establishes that swearing by Allah is the norm. Quranic verses (al-Qalam:17, al-Tawbah:96, al-Munafiqun:1, al-Mujadilah:16) use 'aqsamu', 'ahlifu', 'ashhadu' as oaths without 'billah'.
The Hanafi reading takes these verses as literal evidence of validity.
4
4. Contrast with Zufar and al-Shafi'i
Zufar requires 'billah' for 'aqsamu' etc.; al-Shafi'i requires intention. The Hanafi school rejects these additional requirements, relying on custom and the Quranic usage.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical effect) is that without intention or 'billah', Hanafis still consider it an oath, while Shafi'is do not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واليمين بالله والرحمن والرّحيم وعزته وجلاله وكبريائه وأقسم وأحلف وأشهد وإن لم يقل: بالله، ولعمر الله، وأيم الله . في هذه الألفاظ الثلاثة منهي بصفة أو مجنون؛ لتحقق الشرط، فإن قيل: إن الكفارة لستر الذنب ولا ذنب في المجنون والمغمى عليه والناسي والمكره، قلنا: إن الحكم يدار على دليل الذنب وهو الحنث لا على حقيقة الذنب كوجوب الاستبراء فإنه دائر على دليل شغل الرحم وهو استحداث الملك لا على حقيقة الشغل، ألا ترى أنه يجب وإن اشتراها بكراً أو اشتراها من امرأة، وعند الشافعي في الأصح وأحمد في رواية: لا يحنث بالإكراه وغيره. (محشي، عيني، مستخلص) واليمين بالله: أي اليمين بمعنى الحلف الصادق بالقسم وبالتعليق مشروع بالله وبغيره من أسمائه عز وجل؛ لقوله عليه: "من كان حالفاً فليحلف بالله أو ليصمت"، متفق عليه، أو بصفة من صفاته مثل: الرحمن والرحيم والحق، ولا يحتاج فيه إلى النية، وقيل: كل اسم لا يسمى به غير الله كالله والرحمن، فهو يمين مطلقاً، وما يسمى به غير الله كالرحيم والحكيم والعليم والقادر ، فإن أراد به الله كان يميناً، وإلا فلا، وهذا ليس بصحيح؛ لأن اليمين بغير الله عنه؛ لما روينا، وأما إذا حلف بصفاته كعزته وجلاله ونحوهما، فإن كان متعارفاً بأن كان يحلف به عادة يكون يميناً، وإلا فلا، والأصح وهو اختيار مشايخ ما وراء النهر أن الأيمان مبنية على العرف والعادة، فإذا حلف صفاته التي يحلف بها عرفاً يكون حالفاً، سواء كانت صفة الذات أو صفة الفعل؛ لأن صفات الله تعالى من كلها صفات الذات، وكلها قديمة، ولا يصح الافتراق. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٤/١] (مستخلص) لم يقل بالله : [ أي لم يقل الحالف بالله أقسم أو بالله أحلف أو بالله أشهد (عيني)] أي إذا حلف الحالف بهذه الألفاظ الثلاثة مثل أن يقول: أقسم أو أحلف أو أشهد، ولم يذكر لفظة "بالله" يكون يميناً؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿وَإذ أَقْسَمُوا لَيَصْرِمُنَّهَا مُصْبِحِينَ﴾ (القلم:۱۷)، وقوله تعالى: ﴿يَحْلِفُونَ لَكُمْ لِتَرْضَوْا عَنْهُم﴾ (التوبة:۹٦)، أخبر عنهم أنهم قالوا: ﴿وَنَشْهَدُ إِنَّكَ لَرَسُولُ اللَّهِ﴾ (المنافقون:۱)، ثم قال: ﴿اتَّخَذُوا أَيْمَانَهُمْ جُنَّةً﴾ (المجادلة:۱٦)، وقال زفر: لا تكون هذه الألفاظ يميناً ما لم يقل بالله، وعند الشافعي له: إن نوى ينعقد، وإلا لا. [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٤/١] (فتح)
Oath by Allah, ar-Rahman, ar-Rahim, by His might, majesty, and greatness, and (the words) 'aqsamu', 'ahlifu', 'ashhadu' even if he does not say 'by Allah', and (the phrases) 'la'amru Allah' and 'aymu Allah'. Regarding these three expressions (the oath is valid) from a person who is sane or insane; because the condition (of the oath) is fulfilled. If it is said: the expiation is to cover the sin, and there is no sin for the insane, the unconscious, the forgetful, or the coerced, we say: the ruling is based on the evidence of sin, which is breaking the oath, not on the actual sin, like the obligation of waiting (istibra') which is based on the evidence of the womb being occupied, which is the acquisition of ownership, not on the actual occupation. Do you not see that it is obligatory even if he bought her as a virgin or bought her from a woman? And according to al-Shafi'i in the correct view and Ahmad in one narration: he does not break the oath under coercion and others. (Hashiya, 'Ayni, Mustakhlas) 'Yamin by Allah': meaning the truthful oath by swearing or by suspending (on a condition) is legislated by Allah and other names of His, may He be exalted; due to his (the Prophet's) statement: 'Whoever swears an oath, let him swear by Allah or be silent', agreed upon. Or by an attribute of His, such as ar-Rahman, ar-Rahim, al-Haqq, and no intention is needed. And it is said: every name that is not used for other than Allah, like Allah and ar-Rahman, is an oath absolutely. And what is used for other than Allah, like ar-Rahim, al-Hakim, al-Alim, al-Qadir, if he intends Allah it is an oath, otherwise no. And this is not correct, because the oath by other than Allah is prohibited due to what we narrated. And if he swears by His attributes like might, majesty, and the like, if it is customary that he usually swears by them, it is an oath; otherwise no. And the most correct, which is the choice of the scholars of Transoxiana, is that oaths are based on custom and usage. So if he swears by attributes by which people customarily swear, he is considered swearing, whether they are attributes of essence or attributes of action, because all attributes of Allah are attributes of essence, and all are eternal, and separation is not valid. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 344/1] (Mustakhlas) 'Lam yaqul billah': i.e., if the swearer did not say 'by Allah' but said 'aqsamu' or 'ahlifu' or 'ashhadu' ('Ayni). That is, if he swears by these three words, like saying 'aqsamu' or 'ahlifu' or 'ashhadu', without mentioning the word 'billah', it is an oath; due to His statement: 'And when they swore they would certainly cut it in the morning' (al-Qalam:17), and His statement: 'They swear to you to please you' (al-Tawbah:96), and He told about them that they said: 'And we bear witness that you are the Messenger of Allah' (al-Munafiqun:1), then He said: 'They have taken their oaths as a shield' (al-Mujadilah:16). And Zufar said: These words are not an oath unless he says 'by Allah'. And according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him: if he intends (an oath) it is concluded, otherwise no. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 344/1] (Fath)
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is from the Book of Oaths in the Hanafi madhhab. It begins by listing the expressions that constitute a valid oath: by Allah, by His names such as ar-Rahman and ar-Rahim, by His attributes such as 'izzah (might) and jalal (majesty), and by the words 'aqsamu' (I swear), 'ahlifu' (I take an oath), and 'ashhadu' (I bear witness) even without explicitly saying 'billah' (by Allah). It also includes the phrases 'la'amru Allah' (by the life of Allah) and 'aymu Allah'. The text then addresses a potential objection: why does an oath by a mad person or one under coercion require expiation, given that there is no sin? The Hanafi response is that the ruling of expiation is based on the evidence of sin, which is the mere act of breaking the oath, not on the actual sin. This is compared to the ruling of istibra' (waiting period for a slave woman), which is based on the evidence that the womb may be occupied, not on actual pregnancy. The Hanafi position is contrasted with al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, who say that coercion prevents the oath from being broken. The passage then explains that an oath by Allah or by any of His names is valid, citing the hadith 'Whoever swears an oath, let him swear by Allah or be silent'. It discusses the difference between names exclusive to Allah (like Allah and ar-Rahman) and those shared with creation (like ar-Rahim). The correct view is that even shared names become oaths if Allah is intended, but the stronger opinion is that all attributes of Allah, whether of essence or action, are valid for oaths based on custom. The Hanafi school relies on custom and usage in such matters. Finally, the passage addresses the three words 'aqsamu', 'ahlifu', 'ashhadu' without 'billah'. It cites Quranic verses where these words are used as oaths, and notes the disagreement of Zufar and al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi position is that they are oaths without need for additional words, based on the clear usage in the Quran.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath sworn by Allah or by any of His names or attributes that affirms or denies an action, with consequences for breaking it.
صفات الذات (sifat al-dhat): Attributes of Allah's essence, such as might and majesty, which are eternal and not created.
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath; the act of failing to fulfill what was sworn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, it is an oath.
B) No, it is not an oath unless he says 'بالله'.
C) It is an oath only if he intends it.
D) It is an oath only according to Zufar.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, oaths are fundamentally based on custom and usage – any expression that people commonly understand as an oath is valid and binding.

Valid Oath Phrases: 'La'amru Allah' and 'Aymu Allah'
Linguistic and legal analysis of two oath phrases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that 'la'amru Allah' and 'aymu Allah' are valid oaths and explain the linguistic basis for each.

Core Idea
Custom and linguistic usage make these phrases valid oaths without explicit intention.

Swearing by Allah's existence or blessing may sound indirect, but the Hanafi school recognizes phrases like 'la'amru Allah' and 'aymu Allah' as binding oaths. This lesson explores their linguistic derivation and why custom suffices to establish their legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Oath Phrases
Note the two phrases discussed: 'la'amru Allah' and 'aymu Allah'. Understand that the passage examines both as valid oath forms.
2
Analyze the Linguistic Derivation of 'La'amru Allah'
Recognize that 'amr' means existence or life, an attribute of Allah's essence. The phrase is used with fatha on 'ayn and implies an oath by Allah's existence.
The predicate is omitted obligatorily, meaning 'my oath'.
3
Analyze the Linguistic Derivation of 'Aymu Allah'
Two views: Kufans derive it from 'aymun' (plural of yamin – oaths); Basrans derive it from 'yumn' (blessing). Both views are accepted in the Hanafi school.
The phrase has multiple variants like 'amu Allah', 'mu Allah', etc.
4
Determine the Legal Ruling
Based on linguistic analysis and Arab custom, both phrases are valid oaths. Breaking them incurs the same consequences as a regular oath (kaffarah).
No explicit intention is needed; custom establishes the oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولعمر الله: [ أي واليمين يكون أيضاً بقوله : لعمر الله لأفعلن كذا؛ لأن عمر بقاؤه فكان صفة. (عيني)] بفتح العين أي بقاؤه، وهو من صفات الذات، فكأنه قال: وبقاء الله وبضم العين وإن كان بمعنى البقاء أيضاً، إلا لم يستعمل في القسم؛ لأن القسم موضع التخفيف لكثرة استعماله. وهو مرفوع على الابتداء، والخبر محذوف وجوباً أي لعمر الله قسمي. (فتح) وأيم الله: [ أي وكذا بقوله: أيم الله لأفعلن كذا؛ لأن معناه ويمين الله. (عيني)] واختلف فيه، فقال الكوفيون: أصله أيمن جمع يمين، فحذفت الهمزة والنون تخفيفاً فقيل: أيم الله بفتح الهمزة وكسرها، وربما حذفوا الياء أيضاً، فقالوا: أم الله، وربما أبقوا الميم وحدها مضمومة ومكسورة، فقالوا: م الله، وربما قالوا: من الله بتثليث الميم، فالجميع تسعة أوجه، وقال البصريون: ليست هذه جمعاً، والهمزة للوصل أي كلمة اشتقت من اليمن ساكنة = الله ونقل الحموي أن أيم الله مأخوذ من اليمن وهو البركة فمعنى أيم الله بركة الله قسمي، والعرب كانوا يحلفون باليمن رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٤/١] (فتح)
And (the phrase) 'la'amru Allah': [i.e., the oath also occurs by his saying: 'la'amru Allah la af'alanna kadha'; because 'amr' means His existence, so it is an attribute. ('Ayni)] With fatha on the 'ayn meaning His existence, and it is an attribute of essence, so it is as if he said: 'By the existence of Allah'. And with dammah on the 'ayn, although it also means existence, it is not used in oaths, because the oath is a place of lightness due to its frequent use. It is raised as the subject, and the predicate is necessarily omitted, i.e., 'la'amru Allah' is my oath. (Fath) And (the phrase) 'aymu Allah': [i.e., similarly by his saying: 'aymu Allah la af'alanna kadha'; because its meaning is 'by the oath of Allah'. ('Ayni)] And there is disagreement about it. The Kufans say: its origin is 'aymun', plural of 'yamin', so the hamzah and noon were dropped for lightness, thus 'aymu Allah' with fatha and kasra on the hamzah. Sometimes they also drop the ya', so they say 'amu Allah'. Sometimes they keep only the meem, with dammah and kasra, so they say 'mu Allah'. Sometimes they say 'man Allah' with three possibilities on the meem, making nine forms. The Basrans say: it is not a plural, and the hamzah is a conjunctive hamzah; it is a word derived from 'yumn' (blessing), with sukoon on the meem = Allah. And al-Hamawi narrated that 'aymu Allah' is taken from 'yumn' which is blessing, so the meaning of 'aymu Allah' is 'the blessing of Allah is my oath', and the Arabs used to swear by blessing. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 344/1] (Fath)
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of valid oath expressions, focusing on two specific phrases: 'la'amru Allah' and 'aymu Allah'. The commentator first explains 'la'amru Allah': linguistically, 'amr' means 'existence' or 'life', and it is an attribute of Allah's essence. Swearing by it is like swearing by Allah's existence. The phrase is always pronounced with a fatha on the 'ayn, as using dammah would imply a different meaning not customary for oaths. The grammatical construction is that 'la'amru' is the subject, and the predicate is omitted obligatorily, implying 'my oath'. Then the passage discusses 'aymu Allah'. There is a linguistic disagreement between the Kufan and Basran schools. The Kufans hold it is a contraction of 'aymun' (plural of 'yamin', meaning oaths). The Basrans derive it from 'yumn' (blessing). Both views accept it as a valid oath phrase. The commentator quotes al-Hamawi that the Arabs used to swear by blessing. The Hanafi position is that these phrases are valid oaths by custom and linguistic usage, without need for intention.

Key Terms
لعمر الله (la'amru Allah): An oath phrase meaning 'by the life of Allah' or 'by the existence of Allah'.
أيم الله (aymu Allah): An oath phrase meaning 'by the oath of Allah' or 'by the blessing of Allah', depending on linguistic derivation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, which of the following is the linguistic origin of 'أيم الله' according to the Basrans?
A) It is derived from 'yamin' (oath).
B) It is derived from 'yumn' (blessing).
C) It is a contraction of 'Allah yamin'.
D) It is a foreign word.

Key Takeaway
Both 'la'amru Allah' and 'aymu Allah' are valid oath forms in the Hanafi school, supported by linguistic custom.

Oaths by 'Ahd Allah' and 'Mithaq Allah'
Explaining that covenants and pledges of Allah are valid oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that 'ahd Allah' and 'mithaq Allah' are valid oaths without intention, and explain the Quranic and customary basis for this position.

Core Idea
The phrases 'ahd Allah' and 'mithaq Allah' are valid oaths without intention, based on customary usage and Quranic pairing of covenant with oath.

In the Hanafi school, certain phrases are automatically considered oaths even if the speaker does not intend an oath. This lesson examines two such phrases: 'ahd Allah' (covenant of Allah) and 'mithaq Allah' (pledge of Allah). We will explore the evidence from the Quran and the disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i, who requires explicit intention.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi madhhab considers 'ahd Allah' and 'mithaq Allah' as valid oaths without any specific intention.
The passage explicitly says 'because the covenant is an oath' and 'because the pledge means covenant'.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Recognize that al-Shafi'i requires intention for these phrases to constitute oaths.
The text says 'according to al-Shafi'i, it is not concluded... except with intention'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
Look at the Quranic verse (al-Nahl:91) that pairs fulfilling the covenant with not breaking oaths. Understand how the Hanafi school uses this to establish that 'ahd' is equivalent to an oath.
The verse: 'And fulfill the covenant of Allah when you have covenanted, and do not break oaths after their confirmation.'
4
Follow the Hanafi response to the opponent
Understand that the Hanafi response is based on customary usage ('urf): the word 'ahd' is commonly used to mean an oath, so no intention is needed.
The text states 'due to the prevalence of using 'ahd' in the sense of an oath'.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Note the real-world difference: if a person says 'ahd Allah la af'al' without intention, Hanafis consider it a binding oath (breaking it requires kaffarah), while Shafi'is do not.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position.

(عيني)
And (the phrase) 'ahd Allah': [i.e., likewise by his saying 'ahd Allah'; because a covenant is an oath. ('Ayni)] It is only that 'ahd Allah' is an oath because the swearer covenants to do that thing or not to do it, and he utters it absolutely, so it includes even if he did not intend (an oath) due to the prevalence of using 'ahd' in the sense of an oath. Allah says: 'And fulfill the covenant of Allah when you have covenanted' (al-Nahl:91), then He said: 'And do not break oaths after their confirmation' (al-Nahl:91). And His statement 'and His pledge' because it means the covenant. And according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him: it is not concluded by 'ahd Allah and mithaquhu except with intention. Commentary
This passage addresses the expressions 'ahd Allah' (the covenant of Allah) and 'mithaq Allah' (the pledge of Allah). The Hanafi position is that these phrases constitute valid oaths. The reasoning is that when a person says 'by the covenant of Allah', he is making a covenant to do or not do something, and the word 'ahd' is commonly used in the sense of an oath. The Quranic verse links fulfilling the covenant with not breaking oaths, showing the equivalence. Therefore, no intention is needed for these to be binding oaths. The text records the disagreement of al-Shafi'i, who holds that such phrases require intention to be considered oaths. The Hanafi school relies on the customary usage and the Quranic pairing of covenant and oath.

Key Terms
عهد الله (ahd Allah): A phrase meaning 'by the covenant of Allah', considered a valid oath in Hanafi fiqh.
ميثاق (mithaq): A pledge or covenant; synonymous with 'ahd' and thus also a valid oath phrase.

The Hanafi Ruling on Unspecified Vows (Nadhr) Treated as Oaths
Explaining that uttering 'upon me a vow' constitutes an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on unspecified vows (nazr) as oaths and distinguish between scenarios requiring fulfillment versus expiation.

Core Idea
An unspecified vow is an oath; a specified vow with an unserious condition can be expiated.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi treatment of the phrase 'upon me a vow' (nazr). While a vow usually implies a commitment to perform an act of worship, the Hanafi school holds that if no specific act is mentioned, it functions as an oath, carrying the obligation of expiation (kaffarah) upon breach. We also explore the nuanced intra-Hanafi disagreement over conditional vows with specified acts, culminating in Imam Abu Hanifah's retraction shortly before his death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is uttering 'upon me a vow' (nazr). If no specific act is mentioned, it is treated as a binding oath; breaking it requires kaffarah al-yamin (expiation of an oath).
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'Kaffarat al-nadhr idha lam yusamma kaffarat yamin' (The expiation for a vow when the act is not specified is the expiation of an oath). This is the primary evidence.
Reported by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi, who declared it authentic.
3
Distinguish absolute vs. conditional vows
A vow can be absolute (no condition) or conditional (upon a condition). If no act is specified in either case, kaffarah is required—immediately for absolute, upon condition occurrence for conditional.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
For conditional vows where a specific act is named and the condition is not meant seriously (e.g., said in anger), there is a dispute: earlier view required fulfillment; later view (retracted by Abu Hanifah) allows expiation.
The text states 'wa huwa al-sahih' (this is correct) and mentions the retraction.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The operative ruling is the later view of Abu Hanifah: expiation of an oath suffices for an unserious conditional vow. This is relied upon for fatwa.
Authority: Zahir al-Riwayah with later adjustment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلي نذر ونذر الله : [ أي وكذا بقوله: علي نذر الله، فإن نوى بلفظ النذر قربة لزمته، وإلا فعليه الكفارة.] ولابد من ذكر المحلوف عليه؛ لكونها يمينا منعقدة؛ لأنه بدون تسمية شيء يوجب الكفارة؛ لقوله عليها : "كفارة النذر إذا لم يسم كفارة يمين"، رواه ابن ماجة والترمذي وصححه ثم لا يخلو إما أن يكون النذر مطلقاً أو معلقاً بشرط، وكل واحد منهما إما بتسمية شيء أو لا، فإن لم يسم شيئاً مثل أن يقول: إن فعلت كذا فعلي نذر، يجب عليه كفارة اليمين،سواء كان مطلقاً أو معلقاً، لكن في المطلق في الحال، وفي المعلق عند الشرط وإن سمى شيئاً ففي المطلق يجب الوفاء به، وكذا في المعلق إن كان التعليق بشرط يراد كونه، وإن كان لا يراد كونه قيل: يجب عليه الوفاء بالنذر ، وقيل : يجزيه كفارة اليمين إن شاء، وإن شاء أوفى بالمنذور وهو الصحيح، رجع إليه أبو حنفية قبل موته بثلاثة أيام، وقيل: بسبعة، وكذا لو قال: علي يمين يجب عليه كفارة؛ لأن معناه على موجب اليمين. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٤/١] (فتح)
And (the phrase) 'upon me a vow' and 'vow of Allah': [i.e., likewise by his saying 'upon me the vow of Allah'. If he intends by the word 'vow' an act of worship, it becomes obligatory upon him; otherwise, he must make expiation.] And it is necessary to mention the thing sworn upon, because it is a binding oath; because without naming something, it entails expiation, due to his (the Prophet's) statement: 'The expiation for a vow when (the act) is not specified is the expiation of an oath' - reported by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi, and he declared it authentic. Then the case is either that the vow is absolute or conditional upon a condition, and each of them is either with specifying something or without. If he does not specify anything, like saying: 'If I do such-and-such, upon me is a vow', he must make the expiation of an oath, whether absolute or conditional, but in the absolute case immediately, and in the conditional case at the occurrence of the condition. And if he specifies something, then in the absolute case he must fulfill it, and similarly in the conditional case if the condition is intended to happen. If it is not intended to happen, it is said: he must fulfill the vow. And it is said: the expiation of an oath suffices him if he wishes, and if he wishes he may fulfill what he vowed, and this is the correct view, to which Abu Hanifah returned three days before his death, and it is said: seven days. Similarly, if he says: 'upon me is an oath', he must make expiation, because its meaning is 'the obligation of an oath'. [Rumuz al-Haqa'iq: 344/1] (Fath)
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage deals with the phrase 'upon me a vow' (nazr) as an oath. The Hanafi ruling is that when someone says 'upon me a vow' without specifying a particular act, it is treated as an oath, and breaking it requires kaffarah (expiation) of an oath. If they intend a specific act of worship, they must fulfill that act. The text analyzes different scenarios: absolute vs. conditional vows, and with or without specifying an act. If no act is specified, the expiation of an oath is required. If an act is specified, the general rule is to fulfill it. However, if the condition is not meant seriously (e.g., said out of anger), there is a disagreement: some say fulfill, others say expiation suffices. The text states that the correct view is the latter, and that Abu Hanifah himself retracted his earlier position shortly before his death, adopting the view that expiation is sufficient in such cases. The passage concludes that saying 'upon me an oath' also requires expiation, as it means 'the obligation of an oath'.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow; uttering 'upon me a vow' (nazr) is treated as an oath if no specific act is vowed.
يمين (yamin): Oath; a binding promise invoking Allah, breach of which requires kaffarah.
كفارة اليمين (kaffarat al-yamin): Expiation of an oath: feeding ten poor, clothing them, or fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must fulfill unspecified acts of worship.
B) He must pay kaffarah al-yamin.
C) He must free a slave.
D) Nothing is required.

Key Takeaway
An unspecified vow is an oath; a conditional vow with a specified act can be expiated if the condition was not intended seriously.

Validity of Swearing by Aym Allah, Ahad Allah, and Mithaq
Explaining the validity of three oath formulations from the matn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on three oath formulations and explain why they are considered valid oaths even without intention, using Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Custom oath expressions like 'Ahad Allah' are binding even without intention, based on Quran and Arab usage.

This lesson examines three specific oath formulations recognized in the Hanafi school: 'Aym Allah', 'Ahad Allah', and 'Mithaq'. While these may not be the typical 'wallaahi' form, the Hanafi school considers them valid and binding oaths. We will explore the linguistic basis, the Quranic proof, and the opposing view from the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three oath expressions
The text presents three expressions: Aym Allah, Ahad Allah, and Mithaq. Recognize each as a valid oath formulation in the Hanafi school.
Aym Allah means 'by the blessing of Allah'; Ahad Allah means 'covenant of Allah'; Mithaq is synonymous.
2
Understand the Hanafi basis for validity
The commentator explains that Aym Allah is derived from yumn (blessing) and used by Arabs. Ahad Allah is considered an oath because the swearer makes a covenant, and the term is customarily used for oaths. Mithaq is treated the same.
No intention required due to customary usage.
3
Analyze the Quranic proof
The text cites Surah al-Nahl 16:91, which juxtaposes fulfilling the covenant with not breaking oaths. This implies that covenant (ahd) is a type of oath.
The dalil block shows it's Quran, qati thubut, zanni dalala.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i requires explicit intention for these expressions to be valid oaths. The Hanafi school disagrees, relying on custom and the Quranic link.
Thamarah: practical difference in binding nature.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If someone says 'Ahad Allah' without intention, in the Hanafi school it is a valid oath; breaking it requires kaffarah. In the Shafi'i school, it is not an oath unless intended.
Know the mufta bihi is Hanafi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام الأيمان وأحكامها - الأول اجتلبت له الهمزة للنطق، ونقل الحموي أن أيم الله مأخوذ من اليمن وهو البركة فمعنى أيم الله بركة الله قسمي، والعرب كانوا يحلفون باليمن رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٤/١] (فتح) وعهد الله : [ أي كذا بقوله عهد الله ؛ لأن العهد يمين (عيني)] وإنما كان عهد الله يمينا؛ لأن الحالف عاهد أن يفعل ذلك الشيء أو لا يفعله وأطلقه فشمل ما إذا لم ينو لغلبة استعمال العهد في معنى اليمين، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَأَوْفُوا بِعَهْدِ اللَّهِ إِذَا عَاهَدْتُمْ ﴾ (النحل: ٩١)، ثم قال: ﴿وَلا تَنْقُضُوا الْأَيْمَانَ بَعْدَ تَوْكِيدِهَا﴾ (النحل:۹۱)، وقوله "وميثاقه"؛ لأنه بمعنى العهد، وعند الشافعي له: لا ينعقد في "عهد الله وميثاقه إلا بالنية رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٤/١] (فتح) وميثاقه أي وكذا بقوله : ميثاق الله ؛ لأن الميثاق بمعنى العهد. (عيني)
Sections of oaths and their rulings. The first: the hamza was added for pronunciation. Al-Hamawi transmitted that 'Aym Allah' is derived from al-yumn which is blessing. So the meaning of 'Aym Allah' is: the blessing of Allah is my oath. The Arabs used to swear by al-yumn... And 'Ahad Allah': meaning also his statement 'Ahad Allah' because the covenant is an oath. And it was an oath because the swearer covenants to do or not do that thing, and it is absolute so it includes when he does not intend due to the prevalence of using covenant in the sense of oath. Allah says: 'And fulfill the covenant of Allah when you have covenanted...' then said 'And do not break oaths after their confirmation.' And 'Mithaquh' because it is synonymous with covenant. According to al-Shafi'i, it is not concluded in 'Ahad Allah' and 'Mithaquh' except with intention. And 'Mithaquh' meaning also his statement 'Mithaq Allah' because al-mithaq is synonymous with covenant.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Quran. The commentator explains that the first type of oath is 'Aym Allah', derived from al-yumn (blessing), a common Arab oath. The second is 'Ahad Allah' (covenant of Allah), considered an oath because the swearer makes a covenant. The term 'Ahad' has become so prevalent for oaths that intention is not required. The proof is from Surah al-Nahl (91): 'And fulfill the covenant of Allah... and do not break oaths', linking covenant with oaths. Similarly, 'Mithaq' is synonymous with covenant, so it is also an oath. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, holding that these require intention to be valid oaths, while the Hanafi position is that they are inherently oaths due to custom.

Key Terms
أيم الله (Aym Allah): An oath form derived from yumn (blessing), meaning 'by the blessing of Allah'.
عهد الله (Ahad Allah): Covenant of Allah; used as an oath in Hanafi law.
ميثاق (Mithaq): Pledge, binding agreement; synonymous with covenant, thus a valid oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

From 'alayya nathr' to Kaffarah: The Hanafi Ruling on Unspecified Vows
Ruling on the statement 'upon me a vow' and its variants as oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on a vow when no specific act is mentioned and apply the hadith 'kaffarat al-nathr idha lam yusamma kaffarat al-yamin'.

Core Idea
An unspecified vow is a binding oath; breach requires kaffarah, not fulfillment of a religious act.

In Hanafi fiqh, the word 'nathr' (vow) can function as an oath. The Prophet (peace be upon him) ruled that an unspecified vow carries the same expiation as a broken oath. This lesson unpacks the scenarios: absolute vs conditional, specified vs unspecified, and the retraction of Abu Hanifa on the conditional vow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the statements that constitute vows
The text mentions 'alayya nathr', 'nathr Allah', and 'alayya yamin'. Recognize these as binding expressions.
Intention matters: if the speaker intends a religious act, it is a vow; otherwise, it's an oath.
2
Understand the hadith evidence
The hadith 'Kaffarat al-nathr idha lam yusamma kaffarat al-yamin' is the proof. Note its transmission (Ibn Majah, Tirmidhi) and that it is authentic.
This hadith establishes that unspecified vows are treated as oaths.
3
Distinguish absolute and conditional vows
Absolute vows are unconditional; conditional vows depend on a condition (e.g., 'if I do X'). The ruling differs based on intention.
For absolute vows, kaffarah is due immediately if unspecified.
4
Apply the rule to specified vs unspecified vows
If a specific act is mentioned (e.g., 'fast three days'), it must be fulfilled. If no act is specified, kaffarah applies.
Specified in absolute: fulfillment is wajib. Specified conditional: see next step.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi difference on conditional vows with unintended conditions
If the condition is not intended to happen (e.g., said jokingly), there are two views: one requires fulfillment, the other allows choice between fulfillment and kaffarah. The correct view (mufta bihi) is the latter, based on Abu Hanifa's retraction.
This is an example of legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(عيني) وعلي نذر ونذر الله : [ أي وكذا بقوله: علي نذر الله، فإن نوى بلفظ النذر قربة لزمته، وإلا فعليه الكفارة.] ولابد من ذكر المحلوف عليه؛ لكونها يمينا منعقدة؛ لأنه بدون تسمية شيء يوجب الكفارة؛ لقوله عليها : "كفارة النذر إذا لم يسم كفارة يمين"، رواه ابن ماجة والترمذي وصححه ثم لا يخلو إما أن يكون النذر مطلقاً أو معلقاً بشرط، وكل واحد منهما إما بتسمية شيء أو لا، فإن لم يسم شيئاً مثل أن يقول: إن فعلت كذا فعلي نذر، يجب عليه كفارة اليمين،سواء كان مطلقاً أو معلقاً، لكن في المطلق في الحال، وفي المعلق عند الشرط وإن سمى شيئاً ففي المطلق يجب الوفاء به، وكذا في المعلق إن كان التعليق بشرط يراد كونه، وإن كان لا يراد كونه قيل: يجب عليه الوفاء بالنذر ، وقيل : يجزيه كفارة اليمين إن شاء، وإن شاء أوفى بالمنذور وهو الصحيح، رجع إليه أبو حنفية قبل موته بثلاثة أيام، وقيل: بسبعة، وكذا لو قال: علي يمين يجب عليه كفارة؛ لأن معناه على موجب اليمين. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٤/١] (فتح)
And 'alayya nathr' and 'nathr Allah': meaning also his statement: 'alayya nathr Allah'. If he intends by the word nathr an act of worship, it becomes binding on him; otherwise, he owes kaffarah. It is necessary to mention the thing sworn about for it to be a binding oath, because without naming something it necessitates kaffarah due to his (peace be upon him) statement: 'The kaffarah of a vow if unspecified is the kaffarah of an oath' (reported by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi and authenticated). Then the vow is either absolute or conditional, and each is either with specification of something or without. If he does not specify something, e.g., 'if I do such-and-such, upon me a vow,' he owes the kaffarah of an oath, whether absolute or conditional, but in the absolute immediately, and in the conditional at the condition. If he specifies something, then in the absolute he must fulfill it. Likewise in the conditional if the condition is something he intends to happen. If he does not intend it to happen, it is said: he must fulfill the vow, and it is said: the kaffarah of an oath suffices him if he wishes, and if he wishes he fulfills the vowed act, and this is the correct view; Abu Hanifa retracted to it three days before his death, or seven. Likewise if he says 'alayya yameen', he owes kaffarah because its meaning is 'upon me the consequence of an oath'.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on 'nathr' (vow) as a type of oath. The commentator explains that saying 'alayya nathr' or 'nathr Allah' functions as an oath. If the speaker intends a religious act (qurbah) by the word nathr, that act becomes binding. Otherwise, he owes the kaffarah (expiation) of an oath. The hadith narrated by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi states: 'The kaffarah of a vow if unspecified is the kaffarah of an oath.' This indicates that an unspecified vow is treated like an oath. The passage then details scenarios: If the vow is absolute (not conditional) or conditional, and with or without specifying a particular act. If nothing is specified, the person owes kaffarah. If something is specified in an absolute vow, he must fulfill it. For a conditional vow, if he intends the condition to occur, he must fulfill the specified act; if he does not intend it, there are two views: one that he must fulfill, another that he may choose between fulfillment and kaffarah — the latter being the correct view, and Abu Hanifa retracted to it before his death. Saying 'alayya yameen' (upon me an oath) also necessitates kaffarah because it implies the consequence of an oath.

Key Terms
نذر (nathr): A vow to perform an act of worship; here treated as a type of oath.
كفارة اليمين (kaffarat al-yamin): The expiation for breaking an oath: feeding or clothing ten poor people, or freeing a slave, or fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'If I do X, upon me a vow' without specifying any act, what is required upon breach?
A) He must fulfill a religious act
B) He owes the kaffarah of an oath
C) Nothing is required; it's not binding
D) He must repeat the vow

Key Takeaway
An unspecified vow is a binding oath; its expiation is kaffarah, not fulfillment.

Swearing by Disbelief: Valid Oath or Not?
Ruling on the statement 'if I do X, I am a disbeliever' as a binding oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers the statement 'if I do X, I am a disbeliever' a valid oath requiring kaffarah, contrast this with the Shafi'i position, and apply the ruling to past-tense oaths (ghamus) with the intra-Hanafi nuance.

Core Idea
Swearing by disbelief creates a binding oath because disbelief's sanctity is absolute, unlike sins that can be overridden by necessity.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a critical question: does a statement like 'if I do this, I am a disbeliever' constitute a valid oath? The Hanafi school says yes, while Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees. The dispute hinges on whether the sanctity of disbelief is akin to the sanctity of Allah's name—absolute and never permissible—or like that of sins, which can become permitted under duress. The passage also explores the special case of swearing about the past (ghamus) and the differing views within the Hanafi school. By the end, you will understand the legal reasoning behind these positions and their practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
The act is a conditional statement: 'if I do X, I am a disbeliever (or Christian, Jew, etc.).' The dispute: Is this a valid oath? Hanafi: yes, requiring kaffarah upon breach. Shafi'i: no, it is like swearing by a sin (e.g., adultery) which is not an oath.
Note the difference: the Hanafi view attaches a financial consequence (kaffarah), while the Shafi'i view leaves no legal effect.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof is a hadith from Ibn Abbas: 'Swearing by Judaism is an oath.' The reasoning (ta'lil) is that the sanctity of disbelief is like the sanctity of violating Allah's name—both are absolute and never permissible, unlike sins that can become permissible under duress (e.g., coerced adultery, necessity theft, wine turning to vinegar).
The key analogy (qiyas) is between the sanctity of disbelief and the sanctity of the Name of Allah. Both are non-abrogable.
3
Understand the Shafi'i objection and Hanafi response
Shafi'i: It is a conditional attachment of a sin (disbelief) to an action, analogous to 'if I do X, I am an adulterer'—which is not an oath. Hanafi response: Sins like adultery or theft can become permissible (e.g., under coercion), so their sanctity is not absolute; disbelief is never permissible, so its sanctity is absolute, like Allah's name.
This distinction is the crux: the attribute 'absolute vs. conditional sanctity' determines whether the statement is an oath.
4
Examine the past-tense case (ghamus) and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
If someone swears about the past, e.g., 'if I did that, I am a disbeliever' while knowing he did it, this is al-ghamus (the lying oath). Abu Yusuf: he does not become a disbeliever. Muhammad ibn Muqatil: he does. The preferred view (al-asahh): if the person knows it is an oath, no disbelief; if ignorant and believes the statement actually makes him a disbeliever, then he becomes one because he consented to disbelief.
This shows the nuance: the ruling depends on the swearer's understanding of the statement's nature.
5
Consolidate the practical consequences
The thamarah al-khilaf: Under Hanafi, a valid oath with kaffarah; under Shafi'i, no oath. For past-tense: the swearer does not become an actual apostate if he knows it's an oath, but kaffarah may still be due (depending on whether the oath is considered valid).
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position for future oaths; for past, the cautious position (no disbelief for the knowledgeable) prevails.

فهو كافر: [ أو نصراني أو يهودي أو مجوسي أو بريء من الإسلام.] أي لو قال الحالف هذا القول فإنه يكون يميناً، وتجب الكفارة عند الحنث؛ لأن حرمة الكفر كحرمة هتك الاسم، وقال الشافعي: لا يكون يميناً؛ لأنه تعليق المعصية بالشرط فصار كما لو قال: إن فعل كذا فهو زان أو شارب حمر ونحوه، ولنا ما روي عن ابن عباس ما أنه قال: حلف بالتهود فهو يمين؛ ولأن حرمته كحرمة هتك الاسم؛ إذ لا يحتمل التبديل بخلاف الزنا وشرب الخمر؛ لأنه من يحتمل التبديل فلا يكون كالكفر في الحرمة ومعنى التبديل فيها أن حرمتها تحتمل السقوط كما في الإكراه على الزنا، والاضطرار في السرقة، وصيرورة الخمر خلاً بخلاف حرمة اسم الله؛ فإنها لا تحتمل السقوط. هذا إذا كان في المستقبل، أما إذا كان في الماضي لشيء قد فعله كإن كنت فعلت كذا فهو كافر، وهو عالم أنه فعله فهو الغموس، فلا يكفر عند أبي يوسف له، وقال محمد بن مقاتل يكفر، والأصح أنه إن كان الرجل عالماً يعرف أنه يمين، لا يكفر في الماضي والمستقبل، وإن كان جاهلاً وعنده أنه يكفر بالحلف، يكفر في الماضي والمستقبل؛ لأنه لما أقدم عليه وعنده: أنه يكفر، فقد رضي بالكفر، ولو قال: إن فعل كذا فهو زان أو شارب حمر لا يكون يميناً بالإجماع. (عيني، فتح)
'So he is a disbeliever': or Christian or Jew or Magian or free from Islam. Meaning if the swearer says this statement, it is an oath, and kaffarah is obligatory upon breaking, because the sanctity of disbelief is like the sanctity of violating the name (of Allah). Al-Shafi'i said: It is not an oath, because it is attaching a sin to a condition, like saying 'if I do such, I am an adulterer or a wine drinker' etc. Our proof is what was narrated from Ibn Abbas that he said: Swearing by Judaism is an oath. And because its sanctity is like the sanctity of violating the name, as it does not admit of substitution, unlike adultery and drinking wine, because they admit of substitution (i.e., their prohibition can fall away under duress or necessity). The meaning of substitution in them is that their prohibition can be lifted, as in coercion for adultery, necessity for theft, and vinegar becoming from wine, unlike the sanctity of the name of Allah, which does not admit of being lifted. This is regarding the future. As for the past for something he has done, like 'if I have done such, I am a disbeliever' while knowing he did it, this is al-ghamus (the lying oath). Abu Yusuf says he does not become a disbeliever. Muhammad ibn Muqatil says he becomes a disbeliever. The most correct is: If the person is knowledgeable and knows it is an oath, he does not become a disbeliever, whether past or future. If he is ignorant and believes that swearing makes him a disbeliever, then he becomes a disbeliever in both cases, because he proceeded with the action believing it leads to disbelief, thus he consented to disbelief. And if he says 'if I do such, I am an adulterer or wine drinker', it is not an oath by consensus.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding whether statements like 'if I do X, I am a disbeliever' constitute a valid oath. The Hanafi position is that it is an oath because the sanctity of disbelief is equated with the sanctity of violating Allah's name, both being absolute and not subject to abrogation. The proof is a narration from Ibn Abbas that swearing by Judaism is considered an oath. Imam al-Shafi'i argues that it is like swearing by sin (e.g., adultery) which is not an oath. The Hanafi response distinguishes: sins can be permitted under duress or necessity (e.g., coercion for adultery, necessity for theft, wine turning to vinegar), so their prohibition is not absolute; but disbelief is an absolute violation of Allah's name, never permissible. The passage then discusses the case of swearing about the past (e.g., 'if I did that, I am a disbeliever') known as al-ghamus (the lying oath). According to Abu Yusuf, the person does not become an actual disbeliever; Muhammad ibn Muqatil says he does. The preferred view is that if the person knows it is simply an oath, he does not become a disbeliever; if he is ignorant and believes the statement actually makes him a disbeliever, then he does because he willingly consented to disbelief. Finally, it clarifies that swearing by specific sins like adultery or wine drinking is not an oath by consensus.

Key Terms
الغموس (al-ghamus): A false oath about the past, where the swearer knowingly lies.
حرمة الكفر (hurmat al-kufr): The sanctity/inviolability of disbelief; it is absolute in the sense that it can never become permissible.
التبديل (al-tabdeel): Substitution or lifting of a prohibition, as in sins that become permissible under duress or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on swearing 'if I do this, I am a disbeliever'?
A) It is a valid oath requiring kaffarah
B) It is not an oath
C) It makes the person an apostate immediately
D) It is considered a lie but not an oath

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, swearing by disbelief (e.g., 'if I do X, I am a disbeliever') creates a binding oath requiring kaffarah upon breach, provided the speaker intends it as an oath, and does not constitute actual apostasy.

The Customary Basis for Recognizing an Oath
Ruling on several phrases not considered oaths due to lack of custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for a valid oath and identify which expressions are not considered oaths due to lack of custom.

Core Idea
An oath is valid only if the expression is customarily used for swearing — otherwise it is not binding.

The Hanafi school establishes a key principle for oaths: the validity of an oath hinges on customary usage (al-ta'aruf). This lesson examines a list of common expressions — such as swearing by Allah's knowledge, anger, or mercy — and explains why they do not count as oaths. The guiding rule is that the phrase must be recognized in the community as an oath. Even religious expressions fail this test if they are not conventionally employed for swearing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the expressions listed
List the phrases mentioned: 'by Allah's knowledge', 'by His anger', 'by His wrath', 'by His mercy', etc.
The text also includes punishment, reward, pleasure, curse.
2
State the general ruling
According to the Hanafi school, none of these expressions constitute a valid oath.
The ruling is based on lack of customary usage.
3
Explain the criterion
The criterion is custom (al-'urf). If a phrase becomes customary for swearing, it becomes an oath.
This allows for change over time and place.
4
Note the exception for intention
If the speaker intends an oath by a phrase that could be an oath (e.g., intending the attribute of knowledge), it may be valid.
Intention matters but custom is primary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا بعلمه : [ أي لا يكون يمينا بقوله: بعلم الله لأفعلن كذا؛ لأن العلم يراد به المعلوم. (عيني)] أي لا يكون يميناً لو قال: بعلم الله لأفعلن كذا؛ لأن العلم يراد به المعلوم، فيكون القسم بغير الله حتى لو أراد الصفة القائمة بذاته كان يميناً، وقوله: "وغضبه وسخطه ورحمته أي لا يكون يميناً بهذه الألفاظ ؛ لعدم تعارف الحلف بها، والغضب والسخط - بفتح السين والخاء - يراد بهما العقوبة، ويراد بالرحمة أثرها، وهو الجنة، وكذا لو قال: "وعذاب الله وثوابه ورضاه ولعنته" لا يكون يميناً، فالمدار على العرف حتى لو تعورف به كان يمناً. (فتح) وغضبه: أي ولا بقوله بغضب الله لأفعلن كذا. (عيني) والنبي: أي لو قال بالنبي لأفعلن كذا، لا يكون يمينا؛ لأنه غير متعارف، وحلف بغير الله لحديث "من كان منكم حالفا" الحديث، وعند الثلاثة بالقرآن وكلام الله والمصحف يمين، وعند أحمد الله: أيضاً، ولو قال: إن فعلت كذا فأنا بريء من النبي أو من القرآن أو من كلام الله القديم أو القائم بذاته، ينعقد اليمين بالإجماع، وعندي: لو حلف بالمصحف أو وضع يده عليه وقال: وحق هذا فهو يمين، ولاسيما في هذا الزمان الذي كثرت فيه الأيمان الفاجرة ورغبت العوام في الحلف بالمصحف رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٥/١] ولو قال: أنا بريء من المصحف لا يكون يمينا، بخلاف ما لو قال: أنا بريء مما في المصحف فإنه يكون يميناً، والحاصل: أن كل ما يكون البراءة منه كفر كالقرآن والصلاة يكون يمنياً. (فتح) بالنبي عنه؟ وهو منهي والقرآن: أي ولا بقوله : والقرآن لأفعلن كذا عيني) والكعبة: أي ولا بقوله والكعبة لأفعلن كذا. (عيني) وحق الله : أي لا يكون يمينا لو قال: وحق الله لأفعلن كذا عند الطرفين؛ لأنه يحتمل الحقوق التي على العباد نحو الصلاة والزكاة، وقال أبو يوسف يمين؛ لأن حق الله تعالى حقيقته وبه قالت الثلاثة، وهو المختار عندي، واختار في الاختيار أنه يمين للعرف، ولو قال: بحق الله فيمين اتفاقاً، واليمين بوجه الله ليس يميناً عند أبي حنيفة، وقال أبو يوسف يكون يميناً؛ لأن الوجه المضاف إلى الله يراد به الذات. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٦/١] (فتح) وإن فعلته أي ولا بقوله: إن فعلت الشيء الفلاني فعلي غضب الله. (عيني) أو سخطه: أي ولا بقوله: إن فعلته فعلي سخط الله (عيني) أو أنا أي أو قال: إن فعلت فأنا زان. (عيني) ربوا لعدم التعارف بالحلف بها، بخلاف قوله : هو كافر؛ لأن العادة جارية به. (عيني)
'Not by His knowledge': meaning it is not an oath if he says 'by the knowledge of Allah I will do such-and-such', because knowledge here refers to the known (object). So it would be swearing by other than Allah, unless he intended the attribute subsisting in His essence. And 'His anger, wrath, and mercy': these are not oaths due to lack of customary swearing by them. Anger and wrath (with fat-ha on seen and kha) refer to punishment, and mercy refers to its effect, Paradise. Similarly, 'by Allah's punishment, reward, pleasure, and curse' are not oaths. The criterion is custom; if they become customary, they become oaths. 'And the Prophet': not an oath because not customary, and it is swearing by other than Allah, due to the hadith 'whoever among you swears, let him [swear by Allah or be silent]'. According to the three (other schools), swearing by the Quran, the speech of Allah, and the mus-haf is an oath. And according to Ahmad, also by Allah. And if he says 'if I do such, I am free from the Prophet, or from the Quran, or from the eternal speech of Allah or that subsisting in His essence', the oath is concluded by consensus. In my view, if he swears by the mus-haf or places his hand on it and says 'by the right of this', it is an oath, especially in this time when false oaths abound and common people are inclined to swear by the mus-haf. And if he says 'I am free from the mus-haf', it is not an oath, unlike 'I am free from what is in the mus-haf', which is an oath. The conclusion: everything from which being free is disbelief, like the Quran and prayer, is an oath. 'And the Quran': not an oath. 'And the Kaaba': not an oath. 'And the right of Allah': not an oath according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?) because it could mean the rights due to Allah such as prayer and zakat. Abu Yusuf said it is an oath because the right of Allah is His reality, and the three (other imams) said so too, and this is the chosen view in my opinion. Al-Ikhtiyar chose it is an oath due to custom. If he says 'bi-haqq Allah' it is an oath by agreement. Swearing by the face of Allah is not an oath with Abu Hanifa; Abu Yusuf said it is an oath because 'face' when added to Allah refers to the essence. 'And if I do it': not an oath if he says 'upon me is the anger of Allah'. 'Or His wrath': not an oath. 'Or I am' (e.g., I am an adulterer): not an oath, due to lack of custom, unlike saying 'he is a disbeliever' because custom exists for that.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage lists numerous expressions that are not considered valid oaths in the Hanafi school due to lack of customary usage (ta'aruf). The guiding principle is that an oath is recognized by the custom of the people. Saying 'by Allah's knowledge' is not an oath because the word 'knowledge' is commonly understood to mean 'the known object' (al-ma'lum), so it is like swearing by other than Allah; however, if one intends the attribute of knowledge subsisting in Allah's essence, it becomes an oath. Similarly, phrases like 'by Allah's anger, wrath, mercy, punishment, reward, pleasure, curse' are not oaths because they are not customarily used for swearing. The exception: if they become customary in a particular society, they would be oaths. Swearing by the Prophet, the Quran, the Kaaba, and 'the right of Allah' are also not oaths according to the primary Hanafi view. However, there are disagreements: The other three schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) consider swearing by the Quran and the mus-haf as valid oaths. According to Ahmad, swearing by Allah (i.e., any name of Allah) is an oath. Among Hanafi scholars, Abu Yusuf holds that 'the right of Allah' is an oath because it refers to Allah's very reality, and other schools agree; the author of Fath al-Qadir chooses this view. Swearing by 'wajh Allah' (face of Allah) is not an oath according to Abu Hanifa but is according to Abu Yusuf. The passage also notes that saying 'I am free from the Prophet, Quran, etc.' constitutes an oath by consensus if it implies disavowal of something whose disavowal is disbelief. Swearing by the mus-haf by placing a hand on it and saying 'by the right of this' is considered an oath, especially due to common practice. The general rule: any statement implying disavowal of something that constitutes disbelief (like the Quran or prayer) is an oath.

Key Terms
التعارف (al-ta'aruf): Customary usage; the basis for recognizing a phrase as an oath or not.
العرف (al-‘urf): Custom; the criterion for determining oath validity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for determining whether an expression is a valid oath?
A) Whether it contains Allah's name
B) Whether the speaker intends an oath
C) Whether it is customarily used for swearing
D) Whether it is in Arabic

Key Takeaway
A phrase is only a valid oath if it is customarily used for swearing; otherwise, it has no legal effect.

Disputed Oath Expressions: Prophet, Quran, and the Right of Allah
Ruling on several disputed oath expressions and intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on swearing by the Prophet, Quran, Kaaba, and 'haqq Allah', and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreements and cross-madhhab differences.

Core Idea
Swearing by other than Allah's essential names is generally invalid, but custom can validate some expressions like 'haqq Allah' according to Abu Yusuf.

The Hanafi school holds that oaths must be by Allah directly. Swearing by the Prophet, the Quran, the Kaaba, or 'the right of Allah' is not a valid oath in the primary view. However, there are important disagreements: the other three schools consider swearing by the Quran valid, and among Hanafis, Abu Yusuf considers 'haqq Allah' an oath. The lesson also covers special cases like swearing by the mus-haf with a hand on it, and phrases of disavowal that constitute oaths by consensus.

Guided Path
1
List the disputed expressions
Identify the expressions discussed: 'by the Prophet', 'by the Quran', 'by the Kaaba', 'by the right of Allah', 'by the face of Allah'.
Also includes expressions like 'I am free from...' and swearing by mus-haf.
2
State the Hanafi primary ruling
All these are not valid oaths according to the primary Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).
The proof is the hadith 'Whoever swears, let him swear by Allah or be silent.'
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf disagrees on 'haqq Allah' and 'wajh Allah', considering them valid oaths.
The author of Fath al-Qadir chooses Abu Yusuf's view on 'haqq Allah'.
4
Identify the cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
The three other schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) consider swearing by the Quran and mus-haf as valid oaths.
Their proof: the Quran is the uncreated speech of Allah.
5
Note the exceptions and special cases
Saying 'I am free from the Quran, Prophet, etc.' constitutes an oath by consensus because it implies disbelief. Also, swearing by the mus-haf with hand on it is an oath especially in later times.
This shows that custom and context can change the ruling.

'Not by His knowledge': meaning it is not an oath if he says 'by the knowledge of Allah I will do such-and-such', because knowledge here refers to the known (object). So it would be swearing by other than Allah, unless he intended the attribute subsisting in His essence. The criterion is custom; if they become customary, they become oaths. 'And the Prophet': not an oath because not customary, and it is swearing by other than Allah, due to the hadith 'whoever among you swears, let him [swear by Allah or be silent]'. According to the three (other schools), swearing by the Quran, the speech of Allah, and the mus-haf is an oath. And according to Ahmad, also by Allah. And if he says 'if I do such, I am free from the Prophet, or from the Quran, or from the eternal speech of Allah or that subsisting in His essence', the oath is concluded by consensus. In my view, if he swears by the mus-haf or places his hand on it and says 'by the right of this', it is an oath, especially in this time when false oaths abound and common people are inclined to swear by the mus-haf. And if he says 'I am free from the mus-haf', it is not an oath, unlike 'I am free from what is in the mus-haf', which is an oath. The conclusion: everything from which being free is disbelief, like the Quran and prayer, is an oath. 'And the Quran': not an oath. 'And the Kaaba': not an oath. 'And the right of Allah': not an oath according to the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?) because it could mean the rights due to Allah such as prayer and zakat. Abu Yusuf said it is an oath because the right of Allah is His reality, and the three (other imams) said so too, and this is the chosen view in my opinion. Al-Ikhtiyar chose it is an oath due to custom. If he says 'bi-haqq Allah' it is an oath by agreement. Swearing by the face of Allah is not an oath with Abu Hanifa; Abu Yusuf said it is an oath because 'face' when added to Allah refers to the essence. 'And if I do it': not an oath if he says 'upon me is the anger of Allah'. 'Or His wrath': not an oath. 'Or I am' (e.g., I am an adulterer): not an oath, due to lack of custom, unlike saying 'he is a disbeliever' because custom exists for that.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage lists numerous expressions that are not considered valid oaths in the Hanafi school due to lack of customary usage (ta'aruf). The guiding principle is that an oath is recognized by the custom of the people. Saying 'by Allah's knowledge' is not an oath because the word 'knowledge' is commonly understood to mean 'the known object' (al-ma'lum), so it is like swearing by other than Allah; however, if one intends the attribute of knowledge subsisting in Allah's essence, it becomes an oath. Similarly, phrases like 'by Allah's anger, wrath, mercy, punishment, reward, pleasure, curse' are not oaths because they are not customarily used for swearing. The exception: if they become customary in a particular society, they would be oaths. Swearing by the Prophet, the Quran, the Kaaba, and 'the right of Allah' are also not oaths according to the primary Hanafi view. However, there are disagreements: The other three schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) consider swearing by the Quran and the mus-haf as valid oaths. According to Ahmad, swearing by Allah (i.e., any name of Allah) is an oath. Among Hanafi scholars, Abu Yusuf holds that 'the right of Allah' is an oath because it refers to Allah's very reality, and other schools agree; the author of Fath al-Qadir chooses this view. Swearing by 'wajh Allah' (face of Allah) is not an oath according to Abu Hanifa but is according to Abu Yusuf. The passage also notes that saying 'I am free from the Prophet, Quran, etc.' constitutes an oath by consensus if it implies disavowal of something whose disavowal is disbelief. Swearing by the mus-haf by placing a hand on it and saying 'by the right of this' is considered an oath, especially due to common practice. The general rule: any statement implying disavowal of something that constitutes disbelief (like the Quran or prayer) is an oath.

Key Terms
حق الله (haqq Allah): The right of Allah; in oath context, it may refer to Allah's essence or to obligations due to Him.
وجه الله (wajh Allah): The face of Allah; when added to Allah, it refers to His essence according to Abu Yusuf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which expression is considered an oath by Abu Yusuf but not by Abu Hanifa?
A) بالقرآن
B) بحق الله
C) بالنبي
D) بالكعبة

Key Takeaway
Disputes over oath expressions are common; the Hanafi school relies on custom and the hadith to determine validity, with some expressions accepted by later scholars due to changing practice.

Letters of Oath: Ba and Waw
Definition and usage rules for the two letters of oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the letters ba and waw used in oaths and state the rules for their usage: ba with both nouns and pronouns, waw only with explicit nouns.

Core Idea
The ba' is the original oath letter and can be used with any noun or pronoun; the waw is a substitute restricted to explicit nouns.

In the Hanafi tradition, understanding the precise particles used in oaths is essential for correctly formulating and recognizing a valid oath. The two primary letters—al-ba and al-waw—have distinct rules. This lesson clarifies their usage so you can identify proper oath constructions like 'billahi' and 'wallahi'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two particles
Recognise that 'ba' (ب) and 'waw' (و) are the letters used in Arabic oaths. The passage states that 'ba' is the original and 'waw' is a substitute.
2
Understand the rule for 'ba'
Ba can be attached to both explicit nouns (e.g., Allah) and hidden pronouns (e.g., 'hi' in 'bihi'). The verb of swearing can be mentioned or omitted.
Example: 'billahi' = bi + Allah.
3
Understand the rule for 'waw'
Waw is a substitute for ba but only enters on apparent (explicit) nouns, not pronouns. So 'wallahi' is valid, but 'wahi' is not.
This is a key distinction.
4
Apply the rules
Given an oath expression, identify which particle is used and whether it follows the correct rule. For example, 'wabiLLAH' uses waw followed by ba—though not explicitly covered, the direct attachment rule applies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحروفه: الضمير راجع لليمين بمعنى القسم، وإلا لوجب التأنيث؛ لأن اليمين مؤنث سماعي، وقوله: "الباء" وهي أصل في القسم؛ إذ هي صلة الحلف والأصل أقسم أو أحلف حذف الفعل لكثرة الاستعمال، ومعناها الإلصاق أي ألصق فعل القسم بالمحلوف به تدخل على الظاهر والمضمر كقوله بالله ،وبه ويجوز إظهار الفعل معها، تقول: حلفت بالله، وقوله: "الواو" وهو بدل عن الباء، تدخل على المظهر كقوله : والله ،والرحمن، ولا تدخل على المضمر .
And 'its letters': the pronoun refers to al-yamin in the sense of al-qasm (oath); otherwise it would require feminine because al-yamin is a sam'i feminine. And his statement: 'al-ba' which is the original in oath, as it is the particle of linking; the original is 'aqsamu' or 'ahlifu', but the verb is omitted due to frequent usage. Its meaning is attachment: it attaches the act of oath to the thing sworn by. It enters both apparent and hidden (pronouns), e.g., 'billahi' and 'bihi'. It is permissible to mention the verb with it; you say 'haliftu billah'. And 'al-waw', which is a substitute for al-ba, enters only apparent (nouns), e.g., 'wallahi' and 'warrahman', and does not enter the hidden (pronoun).
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage defines the two main letters (particles) used in Arabic oaths: the ba' (ب) and the waw (و). The ba' is considered the original particle for swearing; it is the preposition that links the act of swearing to the object sworn by (e.g., 'billahi'). The verb 'uqsimu' or 'ahlifu' is usually omitted due to common usage. The ba' can be used with both explicit nouns (e.g., Allah) and pronouns (e.g., 'bihi' meaning by Him). The waw is a substitute for the ba', but it can only be used with explicit nouns, not pronouns. For example, 'wallahi' and 'warrahmani' are valid, but you cannot say 'wahi' (by Him) using waw. This is a grammatical and legal distinction important for recognizing the form of an oath.

Key Terms
الباء (al-ba): The preposition ba, the original letter of oath in Arabic, used with both nouns and pronouns.
الواو (al-waw): The letter waw, used as a substitute for ba in oaths, but only with explicit nouns.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The ba is the original oath particle used with any noun or pronoun; the waw is a substitute restricted to explicit nouns.

Invalid Oath Expressions in Hanafi Fiqh
Lists invalid oath expressions with reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which phrases do not constitute a valid oath in the Hanafi school and explain the criterion of custom ('urf) in determining oath validity.

Core Idea
An oath is only valid if it uses a customary expression invoking Allah; otherwise, it is not binding.

Not every expression that mentions Allah or sacred entities creates a binding oath (yamin). The Hanafi school applies a key principle: custom ('urf). If an expression is not conventionally used for swearing, it does not form an oath, and its violation carries no legal consequences unless the speaker explicitly intends an attribute. This lesson examines the specific phrases listed in the text and the reasoning behind them, including intra-Hanafi and cross-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text rules that certain phrases (e.g., 'by Allah's knowledge', 'by the Prophet', 'by the Qur'an') are not valid oaths. Determine the hukm level: these expressions do not create a binding oath, so no expiation is required upon violation.
The default ruling is that these are not oaths; custom can change this.
2
Locate the dalil
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Whoever among you swears, let him swear by Allah or be silent.' Swearing by other than Allah is prohibited, and the listed expressions fall under that prohibition unless custom makes them equivalent.
The text also uses logical reasoning: e.g., 'knowledge' is understood as the known object, not the attribute.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The hadith restricts valid oaths to using Allah's name or attributes. The listed expressions (e.g., 'by Allah's anger') are either not customary or refer to created things (e.g., Prophet, Ka'bah). The legal link: validity depends on custom and the prohibition of swearing by other than Allah.
Custom can override the default; later Hanafis considered 'by the right of Allah' an oath due to custom.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Yusuf differed on some expressions (e.g., 'by the right of Allah' and 'by the face of Allah'). The three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) consider swearing by the Qur'an or mus'haf a valid oath.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is whether expiation (kaffarah) is due.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position, per al-Marghinani and al-Ikhtiyar, is that 'by the right of Allah' is an oath due to custom. For other expressions, the default remains no, but custom can change the ruling.
The text states 'al-mukhtar 'indi' (the preferred view) is that it is an oath due to 'urf.

Book of Oaths. Sections of Oaths and Their Rulings. Not (an oath) by His knowledge, nor by His anger, nor by His displeasure, nor by His mercy, nor by the Prophet, nor by the Qur'an, nor by the Ka'bah, nor by the right of Allah; and if he says: 'If I do it, then by the mercy of Allah I will do such-and-such' or 'upon me is His anger' or 'His displeasure' or 'I am a fornicator' or 'a thief' or 'a drinker of wine' or 'an eater of usury'... Commentary
This passage lists expressions that are not considered valid oaths (yamin) according to the Hanafi school. The ruling is based on the principle of 'urf (custom): an oath is only valid if the expression is commonly used for swearing. For example, saying 'by Allah's knowledge' is not an oath because 'knowledge' is usually understood as the known object (the known thing), not the attribute of knowledge. If the speaker intended the attribute (the quality subsisting in Allah), it would be an oath, but this is not the default. Similarly, 'by His anger' etc. are not oaths because they are not customary; they are understood as punishment. The author (al-Marghinani) notes that the criterion is 'urf; if such expressions become customary, they become oaths. For the Prophet, swearing by him is not an oath because it is swearing by other than Allah, and a hadith forbids that. The three (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar) consider swearing by the Qur'an, Allah's speech, and the mus'haf as an oath; Ahmad considers swearing by Allah's name as an oath. There is also discussion on 'right of Allah' (haqq Allah): Abu Hanifa does not consider it an oath, but Abu Yusuf does, and the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that it is an oath due to custom. The phrase 'by the face of Allah' is not an oath according to Abu Hanifa but is according to Abu Yusuf. The passage also covers conditional oaths like 'if I do such-and-such, then upon me is Allah's anger'—these are not oaths due to lack of custom, unlike 'he is a kafir' which is customary.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): A legally binding oath that invokes Allah or His attributes, with consequences for breaking it (expiation).
عرف (urf): Customary practice; the criterion for determining whether an expression is considered an oath.

Key Takeaway
An oath is only valid if it is customary to swear by that expression and it invokes Allah or His attributes; otherwise, no legal obligation arises.

Particles of Oath in Hanafi Fiqh
Explains the three main particles and their rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three main oath particles (ba, waw, ta) and state the correct usage rules for each according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Each oath particle has a specific domain: ba is universal, waw is limited to explicit nouns, ta is only for Allah's name.

In the Hanafi school, the formulation of an oath (yamin) must follow precise linguistic rules. The particles used—ba, waw, and ta—are not interchangeable; each has restrictions that affect the validity of the oath. This lesson breaks down these rules from the classical text, allowing you to apply them correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three particles and their status
The passage states that ba is the original particle (asl) in oaths, waw is a substitute (badal) for ba, and ta is a substitute for waw. Note the hierarchy: ba > waw > ta in terms of usage breadth.
Memorize this hierarchy to understand the increasing restrictions.
2
Understand the rules for ba
Ba can be used with both explicit nouns (dhahir) like 'Allah' and implicit pronouns (mudmar) like 'hi'. The verb of swearing (e.g., 'haliftu') may be mentioned alongside it. Example: 'billahi' or 'bihi'.
Ba is the most flexible particle.
3
Understand the rules for waw
Waw can only be used with explicit nouns (e.g., 'wallahi', 'warrahman'). It cannot be used with pronouns. The verb may not be mentioned; you cannot say 'haliftu wallahi'.
The prohibition of the verb is a key distinction from ba.
4
Understand the rules for ta
Ta is even more restricted: it can only be used with the name 'Allah' specifically (e.g., 'tallahi'). It cannot be used with other divine names like 'al-Rahman'. The verb may not be mentioned.
This is the most limited particle; even 'tarrahmani' is invalid.
5
Recognize additional oath letters
The passage mentions other letters that can function as oath particles in certain expressions: lam al-qasm, ha al-tanbih, hamzat al-istifham, etc. These are irregular and used in expressions like 'Allahi' (with implicit oath).
These are less common but must be recognized for comprehensive knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحروفه: الباء وهي أصل في القسم؛ إذ هي صلة الحلف والأصل أقسم أو أحلف حذف الفعل لكثرة الاستعمال، ومعناها الإلصاق أي ألصق فعل القسم بالمحلوف به تدخل على الظاهر والمضمر كقوله بالله ،وبه ويجوز إظهار الفعل معها، تقول: حلفت بالله، وقوله: "الواو" وهو بدل عن الباء، تدخل على المظهر كقوله : والله ،والرحمن، ولا تدخل على المضمر . ولا يجوز إظهار الفعل معها. لا يقال: أحلف والله كما يقال : أحلف بالله، وهو ملحق بالباء؛ لمناسبة معنوية في الإلصاق من الجمع الذي هو معنى الواو، والبدلية انحطت عنها، فدخلت على المظهر فقط. وقوله: "والتاء" وهو بدل من الواو وملحق به، أو حروف الزيادة أبدلت كثيراً منها كتراث وتجاه، هي من فانحطت عنها درجتين، فلم تدخل على المظهر إلا على اسم الله خاصة فلا يقال: تالرحمن، ولا تالرحيم، وجاء "ترب الكعبة"، وهو شاذ، ولا يجوز إظهار الفعل معها، لا يقال: أحلف تالله ولا أقسم تالله، وله حروف أخر، وهي: لام القسم وحرف التنبيه وهمزة الاستفهام وقطع ألف الوصل والميم المكسورة والمضمومة كقوله: الله وها الله وفا الله والله وم الله ومُ الله . [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٦/١] (فتح)

الباء: كقوله بالله لأفعلن وهي الأصل في الباب تدخل على الظاهر والمضمر. (عيني) والواو كقوله : والله لأفعلن، وهو بدل عن الباء، تدخل على المظهر، ولا تدخل على المضمر. (عيني) والتاء: كقوله: تالله لأفعلن، وهو بدل من الواو، ولا تدخل إلا على لفظة الله خاصة. (عيني)
And its particles: the ba' which is the original in oaths; because it is the link of swearing, and the original is 'I swear' or 'I take an oath', the verb is omitted due to frequent use, and its meaning is attachment: I attach the act of swearing to the one sworn by. It enters upon apparent and implicit (pronouns) like 'by Allah' and 'by Him', and it is permissible to mention the verb with it; you say 'I swore by Allah'. And the waw is a substitute for the ba', it enters upon apparent (nouns) like 'by Allah' and 'by the Most Merciful', and does not enter upon the implicit. It is not permissible to mention the verb with it; one does not say 'I swear by Allah' as one says 'I swear by Allah'. It is annexed to the ba' due to a meaning of attachment, from the combination which is the meaning of waw, and its substitutiveness is lower than it, so it enters only upon the apparent. And the ta' is a substitute for the waw and annexed to it, or from the letters of increase, often substituted like 'turath' and 'tijah', it is from... so it enters only upon the name of Allah specifically; one does not say 'by the Most Merciful' nor 'by the Compassionate', and it came 'turb al-Ka'bah' which is irregular, and it is not permissible to mention the verb with it; one does not say 'I swear by Allah' nor 'I swear by Allah'. And there are other letters: the lam of oath, the letter of warning, the interrogative hamza, the cutting of alif al-wasl, and the meem with kasra and damma, like 'Allah', 'ha Allah', 'fa Allah', 'Allah', 'm Allah', 'mu Allah'.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage details the three primary particles (huruf) used for oaths: ba, waw, and ta. Ba is the original particle; it is the one that directly attaches the swearing to the object. It can be used with both explicit nouns and pronouns (e.g., 'billahi' and 'bihi'). The verb (e.g., 'halaftu') can be fully stated. Waw is a substitute for ba; it is limited to explicit nouns only (e.g., 'wallahi') and cannot be used with pronouns. The verb cannot be mentioned alongside it. Ta is a substitute for waw; it is even more restricted, only used with the name 'Allah' specifically (e.g., 'tallahi'). It is not used with other divine names like 'al-Rahman'. The verb cannot be mentioned. There are additional letters like the lam of oath (la), the warning (ha), interrogative hamza, etc., which are used in certain expressions like 'Allah' (with implied oath), 'ha Allah', etc. These are irregular and treated as oaths.

Key Terms
باء (ba): The original particle of oath, used with both explicit and implicit nouns.
واو (waw): A substitute particle for ba, used only with explicit nouns and without the verb.
تاء (ta): A substitute particle for waw, used only with the name 'Allah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is a correct formulation of an oath using the particle waw?
A) أحلف والله لأفعلن
B) والله لأفعلن
C) وبه لأفعلن
D) تالله لأفعلن

Key Takeaway
The particle rules ensure clarity in oaths; ba is the most flexible, waw and ta have specific restrictions that must be followed for the oath to be valid.
