The Oath Particle and Its Expiation
Two rulings: idmar al-harf and kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the validity of an oath when the particle is omitted (idmar al-harf) and state the required expiation (kaffarah) including the methods of freeing a slave and feeding the poor.

Core Idea
An oath is valid even when the particle is implied; breaking it requires expiation by freeing a slave or feeding ten poor, with detailed rules on feeding.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses two aspects of oaths (al-yamin). First, it clarifies that omitting the particle of oath (e.g., saying 'Allah la af'alanna' without the letter 'bi') does not invalidate the oath, as the omission is customary. Second, it details the expiation (kaffarah) for breaking a valid oath, explaining the permissible ways of feeding the poor and freeing a slave. Understanding these rulings ensures proper fulfillment of oath obligations.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between omission of particle and need for emphatic letters
When the oath particle is omitted, the noun can be in nasb or raf', but the emphatic letters (lam and nun) must still be mentioned. This is a legal-linguistic rule.
The passage states: 'It is restricted to implication of the particle because the particle of emphasis (lam and nun) cannot be omitted; they must be mentioned.'
2
Identify the basis for implication (idmar) being valid
The omission of the particle is customary among Arabs for brevity, so the oath remains valid. This is a proof based on 'urf (custom).
3
Recognize the two exceptions: ayman Allah and la'amar Allah
These two nouns always take raf' (nominative) when used in oaths, and the particle is not omitted.
4
Determine the expiation options from the primary text
The expiation is derived from Quran 5:89: freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing them (the text focuses on the first two). The feeding can be done over ten days by giving one poor person half a sa' each day.
The text uses 'tahrir' (freeing) instead of 'itaq' to follow the Quranic wording.
5
Apply the conditions for valid expiation
Feeding can be by tamlik (giving ownership) or ibaha (invitation). If a weaned child is among the recipients, the expiation for that day is invalid and a replacement must be fed. Also, freeing a relative whom one would already have to free (e.g., a parent) does not count as expiation.

And it may be implied: meaning the particles of oath, like his saying: "Allah (I will do such-and-such)" because the omission of the particle is customary among them for brevity. Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage covers two topics. First, the rule when the oath particle is omitted (idmar). If a person says 'Allah la af'alanna' without the particle, it is still an oath because omission is customary. The omitted particle must be compensated by certain letters; otherwise, the noun after it is in the accusative (nasb) or nominative (raf') as a predicate. Two nouns (ayman Allah and la'amar Allah) are always in raf'. The key point is that the implied particle is the original, and the emphasis letters (lam and nun) must be mentioned even when the particle is omitted. Second, the expiation (kaffarah) of an oath. It consists of either freeing a slave or feeding ten poor people. The feeding can be done actually (giving each one full meal) or provisionally (giving one poor person over ten days half a sa' each day). It can be by ownership (tamlik) or invitation (ibaha). If a weaned child is among the recipients, the expiation is not valid for that meal, and an additional poor person must be fed in his place. The expiation is similar to that of zihar (a type of divorce by comparison). The slave can be any slave, Muslim or not, male or female, young or old. The text ends with a note that the kaf in 'kahuma' is used with a separate pronoun, which is grammatically unusual but permissible here.

Key Terms
إضمار (idmar): Implication or omission of the particle of oath, where the noun is taken as an oath by implicit understanding.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation for breaking an oath; an act of worship to compensate for the violation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person breaks an oath and chooses to feed ten poor, is it valid to give one poor person half a sa' of wheat each day for ten days?
A) No, because the same person cannot be counted multiple times.
B) Yes, because each day is considered a new need, so it counts as feeding ten different poor over time.
C) No, because the expiation must be given all at once.
D) Yes, but only if the poor person is not a relative.

Key Takeaway
An oath remains legally binding even if the particle is omitted, and breaking it requires a specific expiation detailed in the Quran, with flexible methods for feeding the poor.

Oath Particles in Hanafi Fiqh
Definition of oath particles and their grammatical rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three primary oath particles in Arabic and state the specific grammatical restrictions for each in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
The ba is the universal oath particle; waw and ta are restricted substitutes: waw for explicit nouns only, ta for Allah alone.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq introduces the three oath particles used in Islamic oaths. Understanding their rules is crucial for correctly phrasing oaths and determining their validity. The commentary by al-'Ayni and others clarifies the nuances, such as the prohibition of using waw or ta with pronouns and the exclusivity of ta to the name Allah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three oath particles
List the three particles mentioned in the matn: al-bā, al-wāw, and al-tā. Recognize that they are the standard tools for forming oaths in Arabic.
2
Analyze al-bā as the primary particle
Note that al-bā is the original oath particle. It can be used with both explicit nouns (e.g., 'Billahi' - by Allah) and implicit nouns (pronouns, e.g., 'Bihi' - by Him). This is its key distinguishing feature.
The Hanafi school holds that ba is the most general and can be used in all cases.
3
Understand the restriction of al-wāw
Al-wāw is a substitute for al-bā but is limited to explicit nouns only. For example, 'Wallahi' is valid, but one cannot say 'Wahi' (by him). The commentator likens it to the waw of conjunction, which requires an explicit noun.
The waw has a lower rank than ba due to its meaning of conjunction.
4
Learn the severe restriction of al-tā
Al-tā is a substitute for al-wāw, but it can only be used with the name Allah (e.g., 'Tāllahi'). It cannot be used with other divine names like al-Rahman or al-Rahim, nor with pronouns. The occurrence 'Tā rabbi al-ka'bah' is considered irregular (shādh).
Ta is ranked even lower than waw, hence its extreme limitation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الأيمان أقسام الأيمان وأحكامها الباء والواو والتاء، وقد تضمرُ وكفارته تحرير رقبة أو إطعام عشرة مساكين كـ هما أي كفارة اليمين تحقيقا أو تقديرا، فلا يقال: و"ك" و "لا" و"ه" مثل ما يقال بك ،وبه، ولا يجوز إظهار الفعل معها. لا يقال: أحلف والله كما يقال : أحلف بالله، وهو ملحق بالباء؛ لمناسبة معنوية في الإلصاق من الجمع الذي هو معنى الواو، والبدلية انحطت عنها، فدخلت على المظهر فقط. وقوله: "والتاء" وهو بدل من الواو وملحق به، أو حروف الزيادة أبدلت كثيراً منها كتراث وتجاه، هي من فانحطت عنها درجتين، فلم تدخل على المظهر إلا على اسم الله خاصة فلا يقال: تالرحمن، ولا تالرحيم، وجاء "ترب الكعبة"، وهو شاذ، ولا يجوز إظهار الفعل معها، لا يقال: أحلف تالله ولا أقسم تالله، وله حروف أخر، وهي: لام القسم وحرف التنبيه وهمزة الاستفهام وقطع ألف الوصل والميم المكسورة والمضمومة كقوله: الله وها الله وفا الله والله وم الله ومُ الله . [ رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٦/١] (فتح) الباء: كقوله بالله لأفعلن وهي الأصل في الباب تدخل على الظاهر والمضمر. (عيني) والواو كقوله : والله لأفعلن، وهو بدل عن الباء، تدخل على المظهر، ولا تدخل على المضمر. (عيني) الله والتاء: كقوله: تالله لأفعلن، وهو بدل من الواو، ولا تدخل إلا على لفظة الله خاصة. (عيني) وقد تضمر: أي حروف القسم كقوله : الله لأفعلن كذا؛ لأن حذف الحرف متعارف بينهم، اختصاراً، ثم إذا حذف الحرف و لم يعوض عنه هاء التنبيه ولا همزة الاستفهام، ولا قطع ألف الوصل لم يجز الخفض، إلا في اسم بل ينصب بإضمار فعل أو يرفع على أنه خبر مبتدأ مضمر إلا في اسمين، فإنه التزم فيهما الرفع وهما: أيمن الله ولعمر الله رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٦/١] والأولى أن يكون المضمر هو الخبر؛ لأن أعرف المعارف هو الاسم الكريم، والفرق بين الحذف والإضمار أن الإضمار يبقى أثره بخلاف الحذف وقيد بإضمار الحروف؛ لأنه لا يضمر في المقسم عليه حرف التأكيد وهو اللام والنون، بل لابد من ذكرهما. (فتح)
Book of Oaths. Sections of oaths and their rulings. The [particles] ba, waw, and ta, and it may be implied. Its expiation is freeing a slave or feeding ten poor, like them, i.e., like the expiation of an oath, in actuality or estimation. So it is not said: 'w-k', 'l', 'h' as it is said 'bik' and 'bih'. It is not permissible to show the verb with them. It is not said: 'I swear w-Allah' as is said 'I swear by Allah'. And it is annexed to ba due to a meaning suitability... etc.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq discusses the particles used to form oaths in Arabic. The matn states that the oath particles are ba, waw, and ta, and they may be implied. The kaffarah for a broken oath is also mentioned. The commentary by al-'Ayni and others explains the rules for each particle. The ba is the original particle and can be used with explicit and implicit nouns. The waw is a substitute for ba and only used with explicit nouns. The ta is also a substitute but only with the name Allah. The commentary also discusses other particles that can be used for oaths, such as lam and ha. Additionally, it explains the case of an implied oath, where the particle is omitted and the noun takes a specific grammatical case. The difference between omission and implication is that implication leaves a grammatical effect.

Key Terms
الباء (al-bā): The primary oath particle, used with explicit and implicit nouns.
الواو (al-wāw): A substitute particle for oaths, used only with explicit nouns.
التاء (al-tā): A particle used only with the name Allah, as in 'tāllāhi'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) 'Wāllāhi la af'alanna' (by Allah) — note: this is valid, but check the phrasing

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the oath particle al-bā is universal, al-wāw is restricted to explicit nouns, and al-tā is exclusive to the name Allah.

Kaffarah for a Broken Oath: Conditions of the Three Options
Commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq on conditions of kaffarah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three expiatory options for a broken oath and distinguish between the valid conditions for freeing, feeding, and clothing, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on minimum clothing.

Core Idea
Expiation for a broken oath requires freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing them; clothing must cover most of the body (Sheikhayn view is preferred).

This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq details the rulings of kaffarah for a broken oath. It explains the three options—freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing ten poor—and elaborates on the conditions for each. A key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the minimum standard for clothing is resolved in favor of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf). Understanding these details ensures that the expiation fulfills the obligation correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three expiatory options
The matn states three alternatives: freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing ten poor. The word 'or' indicates choice; any one suffices. Fasting three days is only allowed if unable to do any of these.
The kaffarah is wajib upon breaking a valid oath; the options are equally valid.
2
Examine the conditions for freeing a slave
Freeing must be complete emancipation ('itq). Inheriting a slave whom you are already obligated to free does not count if you intend it as kaffarah. The slave can be Muslim or non-Muslim, male or female, young or old, but not a mudabbar, umm walad, or mukatib who has paid part of his kitabah.
The text follows the Quranic verse (Surah al-Ma'idah 5:89) in using 'tahrir' (freeing).
3
Analyze the feeding option and its methods
Feeding can be by tamlik (giving ownership of food) or ibahah (inviting to eat). Giving one poor person half a sa' daily for ten days is valid because each day is a separate need. Paying the value (qimah) also suffices. If a weaned child (under age of discernment) is among the ten, it does not count and must be replaced.
The food must be of the staple type: wheat, flour, sawīq, dates, barley.
4
Work through the clothing requirements and the dispute
Clothing must cover 'most of the body' ('ammat al-badan) according to the two shaykhs. Imam Muhammad allows what suffices for prayer (e.g., trousers). The preferred view is that of the two shaykhs. Acceptable garments: waistcloth, cloak, shirt, qaba, mantle, jubba, wrap. Turbans and caps are not sufficient. For women, a khimar (head covering) must be added.
The garment must be suitable for the recipient; if for an average person, it is valid.
5
Apply the rulings to a scenario
Consider a person who chooses to clothe ten poor. He gives each a pair of trousers that covers only the lower body. According to the preferred view, this is insufficient because it does not cover most of the body. He would need to add a shirt or provide a garment that covers the upper body as well.
The same principle applies if he chooses to feed: ensure each of the ten is a valid recipient (not a child).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(فتح) أو كسوتهم كلمة "أو " للتخيير فكان الواجب أحد الأشياء الثلاثة، وقوله: بما يستر عامة البدن، وهذا أدناه عند الشيخين، وعند محمد: أدناه ما تجوز به الصلاة حتى السراويل عنده؛ لأنه لابس شرعاً، وفي الثوب يعتبر حال القابض إن كان يصلح للقابض يجوز، وإلا فلا وقال بعض مشايخنا: إن كان يصلح لأوساط الناس يجوز، وهو أشبه بالصواب، فإذا اختار المكفر الكسوة كسا عشرة مساكين لكل مسكين ثوب أي إزار أو رداء أو قميص أو قباء أو كساء أوجبة أو ملحفة؛ لأن لابس هذه الأشياء يسمى مكتسياً، ولا يجوز العمامة ولا القلنسوة، وهذا إذا كسا رجلاً، أما إذا كسا امرأة يزيد فيه الخمار؛ لأن رأسها عورة لا تجوز الصلاة إذا كانت مكشوفة، ولا يجوز السراويل القصير، ولا قدر ما يستر به العورة الغليظة على قول الشيخين، وهو الأصح. (فتح، عيني) عامة البدن حتى لا يجوز السراويل إلا باعتبار قيمة الإطعام. (عيني (ط))
And its expiation etc.: i.e., the expiation of the oath in the sense of a swearing is freeing a slave. He expressed 'freeing' with the meaning of emancipation following the verse, and to indicate that the condition is emancipation. So if someone inherits a slave whom he would be required to free, and he intends it for expiation, it is not valid. And his saying: 'or feeding ten poor', in actuality or estimation, such that if he gives one poor person half a sa' each day for ten days, it is permissible, considering him as another poor person due to renewed need. Whether the feeding is ownership, by giving each of them half a sa' of wheat, flour, or sawīq, or a sa' of dates or barley; or it is permission by inviting ten poor and feeding them lunch and dinner, it suffices. Similarly, if he feeds them bread without gravy, or if he pays the value of food, it suffices. And if he feeds them lunch and dinner and among them is a weaned child, it does not count, and he must feed one poor person in his place. ... 'like them': it contains the usage of 'kaf' on a separated pronoun, which is not permissible in wide usage, i.e., like freeing and feeding in the expiation of zihar. The comparison is that it is permissible to free a slave absolutely, Muslim or disbeliever, male or female, young or old, and it is not permissible [in zihar] a missing [type]... in zihar, or clothing them with what covers most of the body... 'or clothing them' - the word 'or' is for choice, so one of the three things is obligatory. His saying: 'with what covers most of the body' - this is its minimum according to the two shaykhs. According to Muhammad: its minimum is what suffices for prayer, even a trouser according to him, because it is legally covering. For a garment, the condition of the recipient is considered; if it is suitable for him, it is permissible. Some of our shaykhs said: if it is suitable for the average people, it is permissible, and this is closer to correctness. So if the expiator chooses clothing, he clothes ten poor, each with a garment, i.e., a waistcloth, a cloak, a shirt, a qaba, a mantle, a jubba, or a wrap; because the wearer of these is called 'clothed'. A turban and a cap are not permissible. This is if he clothes a man. If he clothes a woman, he adds a head covering (khimar), because her head is 'awrah, and prayer is not permissible if it is uncovered. A short trouser is not permissible, nor what covers only the heavy 'awrah according to the two shaykhs, which is the most correct. 'Most of the body' - so a trouser is not permissible except by considering its value in feeding.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq commentary discusses the kaffarah for a broken oath. The matn states that the three options are freeing a slave, feeding ten poor, or clothing them. The commentary explains each in detail. For freeing, it must be a complete emancipation; inheriting a slave and freeing him with intention of expiation is not valid. For feeding, it can be done by giving each poor person half a sa' daily for ten days, considering each day a different poor due to renewed need. Feeding can be by ownership (giving food as property) or permission (inviting them to eat). Paying the value is also valid. If a child is present, it does not count. The commentary also compares this to the kaffarah of zihar. On clothing, the requirement is covering most of the body. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (the two shaykhs) require covering most of the body, while Muhammad al-Shaybani allows what suffices for prayer like trousers. The preferred view is that of the two shaykhs. Acceptable garments are listed, and turbans and caps are not sufficient. For women, a head covering is added.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for a broken oath, consisting of freeing a slave, feeding or clothing ten poor, or fasting three days.
تمليك (tamlīk): Transfer of ownership, e.g., giving food to the poor as their property.
إباحة (ibāhah): Permission to use, e.g., inviting poor to eat without ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the Hanafi school, what is the minimum requirement for clothing in the kaffarah of a broken oath?
A) Any garment that covers the head
B) A garment that covers most of the body
C) A garment that covers the ‘awrah for prayer
D) A full suit including turban and cap

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires that clothing for kaffarah must cover most of the body; the view of Sheikhayn is the relied-upon position.

Consecutive Fasting and Timing of Expiation in Oath Kaffarah
Discusses fasting three consecutive days when unable to do other expiation, and the prohibition of expiating before breaking the oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on consecutiveness of fasting and pre-break expiation, explain the proofs, and compare with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
Fasting for oath expiation must be consecutive, and expiation cannot precede the violation—the cause is breaking, not the oath.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies two key aspects of oath expiation: the requirement of consecutive fasting when one cannot free a slave or feed/clothe, and the prohibition of expiating before the oath is broken. The Hanafi school bases these rulings on additional textual evidence and a specific understanding of causality. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the two rulings: (a) fasting must be three consecutive days, (b) expiation cannot be performed before breaking the oath.
These are the positions that the passage defends.
2
Identify the opposing (Shafi'i) view
Note that al-Shafi'i allows non-consecutive fasting and permits expiation with wealth before the oath is broken.
Malik and Ahmad sometimes agree with Shafi'i on these points.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
For consecutiveness: the recitation of Ibn Mas'ud and Ubayy ibn Ka'b adds 'consecutive' (mutatabi'at), treated as a mashhur khabar that can supplement the Quran. For timing: expiation is for a violation (jinayah), and no violation exists before breaking; also the hadith's 'thumma' is interpreted as 'waw' (and), not ordering.
The recitation is not in the 'Uthmani mushaf but is accepted as a sound reading.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i proof
For consecutiveness: the absolute Quranic text (Q. 5:89) does not mention consecutiveness. For timing: the hadith 'If you swear an oath, then expiate your oath, then do what is better' uses 'thumma', which implies order.
Shafi'i also distinguishes between expiation with wealth (allowed early) and with fasting (not allowed early).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response and tarjih
The Hanafi school responds that the added recitation is authoritative, and the causal logic of jinayah prevents expiation before the act. The word 'thumma' in the hadith can be interpreted as 'waw' based on other evidence. This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
The practical consequence: if someone expiates before breaking, the expiation must be repeated after breaking.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن عجز إلخ: أي فإن عجز أحد الأشياء الثلاثة وهي التحرير والإطعام والكسوة صام ثلاثة أيام متتابعات عندنا، وقال الشافعي: إن شاء تابع وإن شاء فرق، فالتتابع ليس بشرط عنده؛ لإطلاق النص، وبه قال مالك وأحمد، ولنا: قراءة ابن مسعود وأبي ما فصيام ثلاثة أيام متتابعات، وهي كالخبر المشهور الذي يزاد بمثله على الكتاب، ثم الفقر واليسار يعتبر وقت التكفير عندنا وعند الشافعي عند الحنث، حتى لو حنث وهو موسر ثم أعسر جاز التكفير بالصوم عندنا، وبعكسه لا، وعنده على العكس رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٧/١] (فتح) متتابعات: لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَصِيَامُ ثَلاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ (البقرة : ١٩٦)، فلا يجوز التفريق ولو بعذر الحيض. (ط، عيني) ولا يكفر قبل الحنث أي لا يجوز تقديم الكفارة على الحنث، حتى لو كفر قبل الحنث ثم حنث بعد الكفارة، كفر ثانياً، وقال الشافعي: يجوز التكفير بالمال قبل الحنث دون التكفير بالصوم والحاصل: أن اليمين عنده سبب الكفارة والحنث شرط، فيجوز الكفارة بعد تحقق السبب. وعندنا: الحنث سبب لوجوب الكفارة واليمين شرط؛ لأن اليمين انعقدت للبر، فلا يكون سببا للكفارة؛ لأن أدنى درجات السبب أن يكون مفضياً إلى الحكم وطريقا إليه، واليمين مانعة فكيف يكون سببا، ووجه جواز التكفير عنده قوله عليه: "إذا حلفت على يمين فكفر عن يمينك، ثم انت الذي هو خير"، رواه أبو داود والنسائي، وهذا صريح في جواز تقديم الكفارة؛ لأن كلمة "ثم" للترتيب، وبه قال أحمد ومالك في رواية. ولنا: أن الكفارة لستر الجناية، ولا جناية قبل الحنث، فلا يجوز تقديمها، وتأويل الحديث إن صح أن كلمة "ثم" فيه بمعنى الواو، ولهذا لا يجب التكفير قبل الحنث، ولو كان كما قاله لوجب التكفير أولاً ثم الحنث بعده مفصولاً؛ =
Then if unable etc.: i.e., if one is unable to do any of the three things, which are freeing, feeding, and clothing, he fasts three consecutive days according to us. Al-Shafi'i said: if he wishes, consecutive; if he wishes, intermittent; consecutiveness is not a condition for him because of the absolute text. Malik and Ahmad also said this. Our evidence: the recitation of Ibn Mas'ud and Ubayy [that says] 'for fasting three consecutive days', and it is like a widely-known report (khabar mashhur) that can be added to the Quran. Then poverty and wealth are considered at the time of expiation according to us, and according to al-Shafi'i at the time of breaking (hanth). So if one breaks the oath while affluent then becomes poor, fasting is allowed according to us; but the opposite is not. According to him, it is the reverse. 'Consecutive': due to the saying of the Most High: 'then fasting three days' (Quran 2:196), so it is not permissible to separate them even due to an excuse of menstruation. 'And he does not expiate before breaking': i.e., it is not permissible to present the expiation before breaking the oath, so if one expiates before breaking then breaks the oath after expiation, he must expiate a second time. Al-Shafi'i said: it is permissible to expiate with wealth before breaking, but not expiation with fasting. The summary: according to him, the oath is the cause of expiation and breaking is a condition, so expiation is allowed after the cause is realized. According to us, breaking is the cause of the obligation of expiation and the oath is a condition, because the oath is concluded for righteousness, so it cannot be a cause for expiation; the lowest degree of a cause is that it leads to the ruling and is a path to it, and the oath prevents, so how can it be a cause? The basis for his permissibility is his saying عليه: 'If you swear an oath, then expiate your oath, and then do what is better' reported by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i, and this is explicit in allowing presenting expiation because the word 'thumma' indicates order. Ahmad and Malik in one narration also said this. Our evidence: expiation is to cover a violation, and there is no violation before breaking, so it is not permissible to present it. The interpretation of the hadith, if authentic, is that the word 'thumma' in it means 'waw' (and), and therefore expiation is not obligatory before breaking; if it were as he said, expiation would be required first, then breaking after it separately.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage addresses two issues: (1) If one cannot do any of the three expiations, he must fast three consecutive days. Hanafis require consecutiveness based on the recitation of Ibn Mas'ud and Ubayy ibn Ka'b which adds 'consecutive'. The Shafi'i school allows non-consecutive fasting, citing the absolute text. The Hanafis argue that this recitation is like a mashhur hadith that can add to the Quran. (2) Expiation cannot be performed before the oath is broken (hanth). The Hanafi position is that breaking the oath is the cause of obligation, not the oath itself. The Shafi'i school allows expiation with wealth before breaking, citing a hadith with the word 'thumma' (then), which implies order. The Hanafi response is that 'thumma' in that hadith may actually mean 'waw' (and), so it does not indicate order. Additionally, expiation is for covering a violation, which does not exist before the oath is broken. The Hanafi school is followed in this ruling.

Key Terms
حنث (hanth): Breaking an oath by doing what was sworn not to do, or vice versa.
متتابعات (mutatābi'āt): Consecutive days of fasting, with no breaks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which school holds that expiation for an oath can be performed before the oath is broken?
A) Hanafi
B) Shafi'i
C) Maliki
D) Hanbali

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires consecutive fasting for oath expiation and prohibits expiation before the oath is broken, based on additional textual evidence and the principle that expiation covers a violation that only occurs after breaking.

6 min
Breaking a Sinful Oath: Duty to Break and Expiat
Ruling that one must break an oath to commit sin and then expiate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an oath to commit a sin, explain why breaking and expiation are wajib, and identify the method of breaking based on the oath's content.

Core Idea
An oath to sin must be broken; breaking and expiating are both wajib.

When a person swears to do something sinful, a conflict arises between keeping the oath and obeying Allah. This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq clarifies that the oath must be broken and expiation offered. The ruling is based on prophetic hadiths that prioritize obedience to Allah over the oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is an oath to commit a sin. The ruling is that it is wajib to break the oath (al-hanath) and wajib to offer expiation (al-takfir).
The word 'yanbaghi' in the matn is interpreted as wajib in Hanafi usul.
2
Locate the dalil
Two hadiths are cited: 'Whoever swears an oath and sees something better, let him do the better and then expiate' and 'No vow or oath in sin or severing family ties.' The first commands breaking and expiation; the second renders the oath invalid for fulfillment.
The second hadith is reported by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i; it is interpreted to negate the obligation of keeping the oath.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The command 'falyati alladhi huwa khayr' indicates the obligation of doing the better (breaking the sinful oath). The phrase 'thumma liyukaffir' indicates that expiation is also commanded. The second hadith explicitly forbids fulfilling oaths in sin.
The word 'thumma' is interpreted as 'wa' in this context, meaning the expiation is not after a delay but simply sequential.
4
Note the conditions
The person must be Muslim at the time of the oath. The oath must be about a sin (e.g., drinking wine, abandoning father, not praying). The oath itself is valid as an oath but not binding.
If the person is not Muslim, the ruling may differ; this is specified for Muslims.
5
Apply the ruling
If the oath was to refrain from something (e.g., 'I swear not to pray'), breaking means performing that act (pray). If the oath was to do something (e.g., 'I swear to drink wine'), breaking means refraining from that act (not drink).
The text clarifies: 'yubashiru al-fi'la in kana al-yamin fi tarkihi, wa la yubashiru in kana fi al-fi'li'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حلف على معصية ينبغي أن يحنث ويكفّر مسلماً أي حال كونه مسلما في يمينه للأمر به والدليل على جواز مجيء "ثم" بمعنى الواو قوله تعالى ﴿أَوْ مِسْكِيناً ذَا مَتْرَبَةٍ ثُمَّ كَانَ مِنَ الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا﴾ تقديره وكان قبل ذلك لأن الأعمال الصالحة قبل الأيمان لا يعتد بها معصية بأن حلف لأشربن الخمر أو ليهجرن أباه أو لا يصلي ينبغي أي يجب عليه لقوله عليه من حلف على يمين ورأى غيرها خيراً منها فليأت الذي هو خيراً ثم ليكفر عن يمينه ولقوله عليه لا نذر ولا يمين فيما لا يملك ابن آدم ولا في معصية ولا في قطيعة رحم رواه أبو داود والنسائي وهو محمول على نفي الوفاء بالمحلوف عليه يحنث أي يباشر الفعل إن كان اليمين في تركه ولا يباشر إن كان في الفعل
And whoever swears an oath to commit a sin, it is proper that he break the oath and expiate, being Muslim: i.e., being a Muslim at the time of his oath, because of the command. The evidence for permissibility of the word 'thumma' meaning 'wa' is the saying of Allah: 'or a needy person in misery, then he becomes among those who believe' (Quran 90:16-17), its meaning: 'and he was before that' because good deeds before oaths are not considered. 'Sin': such as swearing to drink wine, or to abandon his father, or not to pray. 'It is proper' i.e., it is obligatory upon him due to his saying: 'Whoever swears an oath and sees something better, let him do the better and then expiate his oath' and his saying: 'There is no vow or oath regarding what the son of Adam does not own, nor in sin, nor in severing family ties' reported by Abu Dawud and al-Nasa'i, and it is interpreted as denying the fulfillment of the sworn matter. 'He breaks' i.e., he performs the act if the oath was about refraining, and he does not perform if the oath was about doing.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding an oath to commit a sin. The matn states that one should (yanbaghi) break the oath and then expiate. The commentary clarifies that 'yanbaghi' here means obligatory (wajib), supported by two hadiths: the Prophet's command to do the better and expiate, and the hadith stating no valid oath in sin. The condition 'Muslim' is emphasized: the person must be Muslim at the time of the oath. The sin is exemplified by drinking wine, abandoning one's father, or not praying. The person breaks the oath by either doing the opposite of what was sworn: if the oath was to refrain (e.g., not to pray), they perform the act; if the oath was to do something (e.g., to drink wine), they refrain from doing it. This ruling is based on the principle that obedience to Allah overrides the oath.

Key Terms
ينبغي (yanbaghi): In this context, it means obligatory (wajib), not merely recommended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears to drink alcohol, what should he do according to the Hanafi school?
A) Fulfill the oath and then repent.
B) Break the oath by not drinking and offer expiation.
C) The oath is invalid, so no action is needed.
D) He must drink but also give expiation.

Key Takeaway
Breaking a sinful oath is wajib; expiation is also wajib; the method of breaking depends on whether the oath was to do or to refrain.

No Expiation for a Non-Muslim Breaking an Oath
Stating that a non-Muslim has no obligation of expiation for an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on expiation for a non-Muslim's broken oath, cite the Quranic proof, and contrast it with the Shafi'i position, explaining the practical difference.

Core Idea
A non-Muslim's oath does not require expiation because expiation is an act of worship and non-Muslims lack qualification.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute: does a non-Muslim owe expiation (kaffarah) after breaking an oath? The Hanafi school says no, grounding their view in Quran 9:12 and the nature of expiation as an act of worship. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing that expiation is also a penalty for violation. Understanding this dispute illuminates how the schools prioritize different aspects of a ruling's essence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the exact ruling: no expiation is required from a non-Muslim for breaking an oath, even if the oath is intentionally broken.
The Arabic phrase 'ولا كفارة على كافر' is the core statement.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
Analyze the Quranic verse (9:12) 'they have no oaths' and the reasoning that expiation is an act of worship. Explain why a non-Muslim is not a qualified subject for worship.
The Hanafi legal maxim: acts of worship require faith (iman).
3
State the opposing position
Present the Shafi'i (and Ahmad) view: the non-Muslim must give expiation by wealth (but not fasting) because breaking an oath is a violation that incurs a penalty, and non-Muslims are subject to worldly penalties.
Shafi'i distinguishes between worship and penal elements.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
See how the Hanafis counter: expiation has a dual nature, but the worship aspect dominates. Therefore, the non-Muslim's lack of capacity for worship voids the obligation entirely.
The response also extends to apostasy and vows of obedience.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: in the Hanafi school, no action is taken; in the Shafi'i school, the non-Muslim must give kaffarah by wealth. This affects how judges rule in mixed communities.
The difference is real and applicable in multi-faith societies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا كفارة على كافر وإن حنث لعدم أهلية لليمين؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّهُمْ لا أَيْمَانَ لَهُمْ﴾ (التوبة:١٢)، وقال الشافعي: عليه الكفارة بالمال دون الصوم؛ لأنها مؤاخذة وجبت لما تحقق من الهتك والكافر أهل للمؤاخذات، وبه قال أحمد، ولنا: أن الكفارة عبادة في ذاتها وعقوبة بالنظر إلى سببها، والكافر ليس أهلا للعبادة، ولا لحكمها، فلا تشرع في حقه أصلا وعن هذا قلنا: إنه لو ارتد بعد ما حلف مسلما ثم أسلم ،فحنث لا تلزمه الكفارة، وكذا لو نذر ما هو قربة من صدقة أو صوم لا يلزمه شيء؛ لأنه ليس بأهل للقربات. (عيني، فتح)
And no expiation is due upon a non-Muslim even if he breaks an oath, due to lack of qualification for the oath; because of Allah's saying: 'They have no oaths' (Quran 9:12). Al-Shafi'i said: expiation by wealth is due upon him, but not by fasting, because it is a penalty that became obligatory due to the actual violation, and a non-Muslim is qualified for penalties, and Ahmad agreed. Our evidence: expiation is an act of worship in itself and a punishment in view of its cause, and a non-Muslim is not qualified for worship nor its ruling, so it is not prescribed for him at all. Based on this, we say: if he apostatized after swearing while Muslim then became Muslim, and broke the oath, expiation is not obligatory upon him. Similarly, if he vowed an act of obedience like charity or fasting, nothing is due upon him because he is not qualified for acts of worship. ('Aynī, Fath)
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding whether a non-Muslim must offer expiation (kaffarah) after breaking an oath. The Hanafi position is that no expiation is required, based on Quran 9:12 which states that disbelievers have no valid oaths (i.e., their oaths are not binding in a way that requires expiation). Additionally, expiation is fundamentally an act of worship ('ibadah), and a non-Muslim is not a qualified subject for worship. Imam al-Shafi'i differentiates: he holds that expiation by wealth is due because it is a penalty for an actual violation, and non-Muslims are subject to penalties. The Hanafis respond by emphasizing that expiation is both worship and penalty; the worship aspect disqualifies the non-Muslim. The extension to apostasy is given: if a Muslim swore, then apostatized, then returned to Islam and broke the oath, no expiation is due because during the period of apostasy he lacked the capacity for worship.

Key Terms
لا أيمان لهم (lā aymāna lahum): Literally 'no oaths for them'; interpreted by Hanafis as meaning their oaths are not legally binding in terms of expiation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a non-Muslim swears an oath and then breaks it, what is required?
A) He must offer expiation by freeing a slave.
B) He must offer expiation by feeding the poor.
C) No expiation is necessary.
D) He must fast three days.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that a non-Muslim's broken oath does not incur expiation because expiation is an act of worship requiring Islamic faith.

10 min
Declaring Lawful Property 'Forbidden' as an Oath
Ruling that declaring lawful property 'forbidden' does not make it forbidden but creates an oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that declaring lawful property 'forbidden' constitutes an oath requiring expiation, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Declaring lawful property 'forbidden' is not a genuine prohibition but an oath; violation requires expiation.

Have you ever heard someone say, 'This is haram for me' to avoid something lawful? In Hanafi fiqh, such a statement carries legal weight—not as a real prohibition, but as an oath. This lesson explores the Quranic basis for this ruling and contrasts it with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the person says and what the Hanafi school rules: saying 'my property is forbidden to me' does not make it inherently forbidden, but creates an oath. If he later uses it, expiation (kaffarah) is required.
The act is a verbal declaration, not a physical action.
2
Locate the Hanafi dalil
Find the Quranic evidence: Surah al-Tahrim (66:1-2) where Allah rebukes the Prophet for forbidding something lawful and then ordains expiation for oaths. This shows that forbidding the lawful is treated as an oath.
The dalil is naqli (textual) and explicit.
3
Note the Shafi'i objection
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that such a statement is ineffective because one cannot change the Shari'a; no oath is constituted except in cases of wives and slave-girls (where divorce or emancipation may be implied).
This objection focuses on the statement being a null attempt.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond by pointing to the Quranic context: the verse directly links forbidding the lawful to expiation of oaths. Additionally, an oath can be a negative act (swearing not to do something), and forbidding the lawful is equivalent to swearing not to use it.
The response uses tafsir of the verse and qiyas (analogy).
5
Determine the practical consequence
Thamarah al-khilaf: In the Hanafi school, the person must expiate if he uses the property. In the Shafi'i school, no expiation is required, but if the statement involves his wife or slave-girl, there may be implications of divorce or emancipation.
This shows that the ikhtilaf has real-world effects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حرم ملكه لم يحرم، وإن استباحه كفر. أي حرم على نفسه شيئاً مما يملكه بأن قال: ثوبي هذا علي حرام. لم يحرم أي لم يحرم عليه؛ لأنه قلب الشرع، ولا قدرة له على ذلك. (ط) أي لم يحرم ولا يرد ما استشكله به بعضهم بأنه لما لم يحرم لم يصح فيه لفظ الاستباحة، ولا وجوب التكفير؛ لأن لفظ الاستباحة إنما يذكر عند تناول الحرام، وكذلك وجوب الكفارة إنما يذكر عند ارتكاب الحرام؛ لأنا نقول: إن المراد بقوله: "لم يحرم" لعينه فلا ينافي ثبوت الحرمة لغيره، وهو اليمين الثابتة وكذا لا يحرم لو قال ملك فلان أو ماله علي حرام، ويكون يمينا، فقول المصنف في شروع المسألة: ومن حرم ملكه ليس بقيد بل وقع اتفاقاً. (فتح، عيني) وإن استباحه: أي إن أقدم على ما حرمه وطلب أن يكون مباحاً له كما كان (ط، عيني) كفر: لأن انعقد به يميناً فصار حراماً لغيره. (عيني) أي من حرم حلالاً ثم أراد أن يكون مباحاً له كما كان، كفر ليمينه؛ لأن تحريم الحلال يمين، وقال الشافعي مه لا كفارة عليه؛ لأنه قلب الموضوع على ما ذكرنا؛ فلا ينعقد به اليمين إلا في النساء والجواري، ولنا : قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللهُ لَكَ ﴾ (التحريم: ۱)، ثم قال: ﴿وَقَدْ فَرَضَ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ تَحِلَّةَ أَيْمَانِكُمْ (التحريم: ٢). [تبيين الحقائق: ٣٤٧/٣] ولأن اليمين إن كان على فعل وجودي فهو إيجاب المباح، وإن كان على فعل عدمي كلا أفعل كذا فهو تحريم الحلال، فكيف يقال تحريم الحلال ليس بيمين. (مستخلص، فتح)
And whoever forbids his property, it does not become forbidden, and if he makes it permissible for himself, he must expiate. i.e., he forbids upon himself something he owns by saying: 'This garment of mine is forbidden to me.' It does not become forbidden: i.e., it is not forbidden upon him because he reverses the Shari'a, and he has no power over that. (T) i.e., it is not forbidden, and the objection of some that since it is not forbidden, the term 'making permissible' is not valid, nor the obligation of expiation, because the word 'istibaha' is only mentioned when consuming something forbidden, and similarly the obligation of expiation is only mentioned when committing a forbidden act. We say: the meaning of his saying 'it does not become forbidden' refers to its essence, so it does not negate the establishment of prohibition for something else, which is the established oath. Similarly, it does not become forbidden if he says 'the property of so-and-so or his wealth is forbidden to me', and it becomes an oath. So the author's wording at the beginning of the issue 'And whoever forbids his property' is not a condition but rather occurred by chance. (Fath, 'Aynī) 'And if he makes it permissible': i.e., if he proceeds to what he forbade and seeks that it be permissible for him as before (T, 'Aynī) 'he expiates': because an oath is concluded thereby, so it becomes forbidden for something else. ('Aynī) i.e., whoever forbids something lawful then wants it to be permissible for him as before, he expiates for his oath, because forbidding the lawful is an oath. Al-Shafi'i said: no expiation upon him because he reversed the subject as we mentioned; so no oath is concluded except regarding women and slave-girls. Our evidence: the saying of Allah: 'O Prophet, why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?' (Quran 66:1), then said: 'Allah has ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths' (Quran 66:2). [Tabyīn al-Ḥaqāʾiq 3:347] And because the oath, if it is about an existential act, it is commanding the permissible, and if about a non-existential act like not doing something, it is forbidding the permissible. So how can it be said that forbidding the permissible is not an oath? (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the legal effect of a person saying 'my property is forbidden to me.' The Hanafi position is that such a statement does not actually make the property forbidden (haram) because a person cannot change the divine ruling. Instead, it constitutes an implied oath (yamin), and if the person later uses the property, he must offer expiation for breaking the oath. The Shafi'i view, as cited, holds that no expiation is necessary because the statement is ineffective and does not create an oath, except in the case of wives and slave-girls (where verbal divorce or emancipation may be implied). The Hanafis base their argument on Quran 66:1-2, where the Prophet's forbidding of something lawful is linked to expiation of oaths, indicating that declaring the lawful forbidden is treated as an oath. The commentary also addresses an objection: if the property is not actually forbidden, how can there be a need for 'making it permissible' (istibaha) and expiation? The response is that the property is not forbidden in essence, but the oath has created a non-essential prohibition (haram li-ghayrihi), so expiation is required to remove the restriction of the oath.

Key Terms
حرام لغيره (ḥarām li-ghayrihi): Forbidden due to an external factor (the oath), not inherently forbidden.
تحريم الحلال (taḥrīm al-ḥalāl): Declaring something lawful as forbidden; in Hanafi fiqh, this is considered an implied oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone says 'this garment is forbidden to me' and then wears it, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) He has committed a sin by wearing a forbidden garment.
B) He must offer expiation for breaking an oath.
C) Nothing; his statement is invalid.
D) He must free a slave.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, declaring lawful property 'forbidden' is not a genuine prohibition but an implied oath; violation requires expiation.

Tahrim al-Halal: Forbidding the Permissible as an Oath
Ruling on tahrim al-halal: declaring lawful property forbidden

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on declaring halal haram, explain why it constitutes an oath, and apply the ruling to a new scenario.

Core Idea
Declaring halal haram does not change the object's ruling; it creates an oath requiring expiation if broken.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses a common misunderstanding: when someone says 'my property is haram for me,' does the property become actually haram? The matn and commentary clarify that it remains halal in essence, but the statement is treated as an oath. We will explore the Hanafi evidence and the dispute with al-Shafi'i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its apparent ruling
Read the matn: 'Whoever forbids his property, it is not forbidden, and if he makes it permissible, he must expiate.' Note that the property does not become inherently haram, yet using it requires expiation. Why? Because the statement is an oath.
The act is declaring a halal thing haram for oneself.
2
Understand the distinction between essence and obligation
The commentator explains that 'not forbidden' refers to the essence (la li-aynihi), but a prohibition arises from the oath (li-ghayrihi). The object remains halal in itself, but the person becomes obligated not to use it without expiation.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.
3
Examine the Hanafi proof from Quran and logic
The Hanafis cite Surah At-Tahrim (66:1-2) where Allah rebukes the Prophet for forbidding what is halal and then mentions dissolution of oaths. This indicates that forbidding halal is an oath. Additionally, any oath of abstinence (e.g., 'I will not do X') is tahrim al-halal.
The Quranic evidence is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala; the Hanafi interpretation is supported by usul.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position and determine practical consequence
Al-Shafi'i holds that tahrim al-halal is not an oath except for wives and slave girls. The thamarah al-khilaf: if someone says 'my food is haram' then eats, Hanafi requires kaffara; Shafi'i does not. The Hanafi view is mufta bihi.
This ikhtilaf is legitimate and based on different understandings of the same Quranic verses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن حرم ملكه لم يحرم، وإن استباحه كفر. أي لم يحرم؛ لأنه قلب الشرع، ولا قدرة له على ذلك. (ط) أي لم يحرم ولا يرد ما استشكله به بعضهم بأنه لما لم يحرم لم يصح فيه لفظ الاستباحة، ولا وجوب التكفير؛ لأن لفظ الاستباحة إنما يذكر عند تناول الحرام، وكذلك وجوب الكفارة إنما يذكر عند ارتكاب الحرام؛ لأنا نقول: إن المراد بقوله: "لم يحرم" لعينه فلا ينافي ثبوت الحرمة لغيره، وهو اليمين الثابتة وكذا لا يحرم لو قال ملك فلان أو ماله علي حرام، ويكون يمينا، فقول المصنف في شروع المسألة: ومن حرم ملكه ليس بقيد بل وقع اتفاقاً. (فتح، عيني) وإن استباحه: أي إن أقدم على ما حرمه وطلب أن يكون مباحاً له كما كان (ط، عيني) كفر: [لأنه انعقد به يميناً فصار حراماً لغيره. (عيني)] أي من حرم حلالاً ثم أراد أن يكون مباحاً له كما كان، كفر ليمينه؛ لأن تحريم الحلال يمين، وقال الشافعي مه لا كفارة عليه؛ لأنه قلب الموضوع على ما ذكرنا؛ فلا ينعقد به اليمين إلا في النساء والجواري، ولنا : قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ لِمَ تُحَرِّمُ مَا أَحَلَّ اللهُ لَكَ ﴾ (التحريم: ۱)، ثم قال: ﴿وَقَدْ فَرَضَ اللَّهُ لَكُمْ تَحِلَّةَ أَيْمَانِكُمْ (التحريم: ٢). [تبيين الحقائق: ٣٤٧/٣] ولأن اليمين إن كان على فعل وجودي فهو إيجاب المباح، وإن كان على فعل عدمي كلا أفعل كذا فهو تحريم الحلال، فكيف يقال تحريم الحلال ليس بيمين. (مستخلص، فتح)
And whoever forbids his property, it is not forbidden, and if he makes it permissible [i.e., uses it], he must expiate. Meaning it is not forbidden; because that would be overturning the Shari'a, and he has no power over that. (T) Meaning it is not forbidden, and don't consider the objection of some who say that since it is not forbidden, the word 'istibaha' (making permissible) is not correct, nor the obligation of expiation; because the word 'istibaha' is only mentioned when partaking of what is forbidden, and similarly the obligation of expiation is only mentioned when committing a forbidden act. We say: What is meant by his statement 'it is not forbidden' is in itself, so it does not negate the establishment of prohibition for another reason, which is the established oath. Similarly, it is not forbidden if he says: 'So-and-so's property is forbidden to me' or 'his wealth is forbidden to me', and it becomes an oath. So the author's statement at the beginning of the issue: 'Whoever forbids his property' is not a condition but happened coincidentally. (Fath, 'Ayni) 'And if he makes it permissible' i.e. if he proceeds to what he forbade and seeks that it be permissible for him as it was (T, 'Ayni), 'he must expiate' [because it is established as an oath, so it becomes forbidden due to something else. ('Ayni)] i.e. whoever forbids something permissible then wants it to be permissible for him as before, he must expiate due to his oath, because forbidding the permissible is an oath. Al-Shafi'i said: There is no expiation on him, because it is distortion of the subject as we mentioned; thus the oath is not established except in the case of women and slave girls. And for us is the statement of Allah: 'O Prophet, why do you forbid what Allah has made lawful for you?' (At-Tahrim 1), then He said: 'Allah has already ordained for you the dissolution of your oaths' (At-Tahrim 2). [Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 3/347] And because an oath, if it is about an existential action, it is making the permissible obligatory, and if it is about a non-existential action like 'I will not do such-and-such', it is forbidding the permissible. So how can it be said that forbidding the permissible is not an oath? (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi ruling on a person who says 'my property is haram for me.' The matn states: the property does not become inherently forbidden; but if he then uses it, he must expiate (kaffara) because his statement is considered an oath. The commentator clarifies that forbidding one's own property does not change its legal status (it remains halal) because changing the Shari'a is beyond human power. However, the statement is treated as an oath: by declaring it haram, he binds himself to an implicit oath. If he later uses it, he has broken the oath and must expiate. The Hanafis distinguish between the thing itself (not becoming haram) and the oath (which makes it haram due to the oath, i.e., it becomes sinful to use it without expiation). The text also addresses a potential objection: if it's not haram, why use the term 'istibaha' (making permissible) and require expiation? The response is that 'not haram' refers to the essence, not the personal obligation; the oath creates a personal prohibition. The passage then contrasts with al-Shafi'i's view: al-Shafi'i holds that tahrim al-halal (declaring halal as haram) does not constitute an oath except in the case of wives and slave girls, so no expiation is needed. The Hanafi proof is from Surah At-Tahrim, where Allah rebukes the Prophet for forbidding what is halal and then mentions the dissolution of oaths, indicating that forbidding the halal is an oath. Furthermore, logically, an oath to do something is making permissible acts obligatory, and an oath not to do something is forbidding the permissible; so any tahrim of halal is effectively an oath. The passage demonstrates Hanafi use of Quran and istihsan in expanding the concept of oath.

Key Terms
تحريم الحلال (tahrim al-halal): The act of declaring something that is legally permissible to be forbidden for oneself, such as saying 'my property is haram for me'.
استباحة (istibaha): Making something permissible again, i.e., using the previously declared haram property or action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The food becomes haram, and he has sinned.
B) He must not eat it, and no expiation is needed.
C) He may eat it without any consequence.
D) He must expiate (kaffara) for breaking an oath.

Key Takeaway
Declaring halal haram does not alter the thing's ruling but creates an oath, and breaking it requires kaffara.

Rulings on Vows (Nadhr): Absolute and Conditional
Ruling on vows: definition, conditions, types, and fulfillment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions for a valid vow, distinguish between absolute and conditional vows, and apply the rule that specified details are not binding.

Core Idea
A valid vow must be of an obligatory worship; only the core act is binding, not its specifics.

Vows (nadhr) are personal commitments to perform an act of worship. The Hanafi school treats them seriously but with clear limits: they must be of a type that is generally obligatory, not sinful, not already required, and within one's capacity. This lesson unpacks the conditions and the critical distinction between absolute and conditional vows, as well as the flexibility regarding specified details.

Guided Path
1
Define the vow and its legal nature
Understand that a vow is a self-imposed obligation. It is not enforceable by the judge (qadi) because there is no human plaintiff; it is a matter between the person and Allah.
The text says 'لا يدخل تحت الحكم' – not subject to judicial enforcement.
2
Identify the five conditions for validity
The commentary lists conditions: (1) the vowed act must be of a type generally obligatory (like salah, sawm, sadaqah); (2) it must be an intended act of worship (not wudu or shrouding); (3) it must not be inherently sinful; (4) it must not already be obligatory; (5) the commitment must not exceed one's capacity.
If any condition is missing, the vow is invalid and not binding.
3
Distinguish absolute vs conditional vows
An absolute vow (mutlaq) is binding immediately upon utterance. A conditional vow (mu'allaq bi-shart) becomes binding only when the condition occurs.
Example: 'Upon me is to fast for a year' (absolute). 'If Allah heals my son, I will fast' (conditional).
4
Analyze the rule on specification (ta'yin)
If the vow specifies details like a particular dirham, poor person, or place, those specifications are not binding. Only the core act (e.g., charity) must be fulfilled. The proof is the hadith: 'Whoever makes a vow and specifies, he must fulfill what he specified.' The 'what he specified' refers to the type of worship, not every detail.
The text: 'لا يلزمه إلا أصل القربة' – only the core act of worship is binding.
5
Understand the expiation for unspecified vows
If the person makes a vow without specifying any act (e.g., 'I make a vow'), then it is treated like an oath and requires kaffara yamin (expiation of an oath). This applies whether the vow was absolute or conditional.
The hadith implies specification; without it, the vow is incomplete and expiation is due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن نذر نذراً مطلقاً أو معلقاً بشرط ووجد وفى به . النذر إيجاب الإنسان على نفسه شيئاً، ولا يدخل تحت الحكم فلا يجبره القاضي؛ لأنه لا مطالب له في الدنيا، ويشترط لصحة النذر أن يكون من جنسه واجب، وأن يكون عبادة مقصودة كصوم وصلاة وصدقة، فخرج الوضوء وتكفين الميت، وأن لا يكون معصية لذاته، وأن لا يكون واجباً عليه قبل النذر، وأن لا يكون ما التزمه أكثر مما يملكه، فلا يلزم الناذر ما ليس من جنسه فرض كعيادة مريض وتشييع جنازة ودخول مسجد . (فتح) مطلقاً : أي عن ذكر الشرط، ولم يقيد بشرط بأن قال: الله علي صوم سنة بدون التعليق بشيء. (عيني) بشرط: بأن قال مثلا: إن شفى الله مريضي فلله علي كذا عيني) وفى به أي عليه الوفاء به وجوبا، ويلزمه الوفاء بأصل القربة التي التزمها لا بكل وصف التزمه؛ لأنه لو عين درهما أو فقيراً أو مكانا للتصدق أو للصلاة فإن التعيين ليس بلازم، ووجوب الوفاء؛ لقوله عليه: "من نذر وسمى فعليه الوفاء بما سمى"، وهذا إذا سمى وإن لم يسم فعليه كفارة يمين سواء كان النذر مطلقاً أو معلقاً، لكن يجب في المطلق في الحال وفي المعلق عند وجود الشرط. (فتح، مستخلص، عيني)
And whoever makes a vow, whether absolute or conditional and it occurs, he must fulfill it. A vow is a person making something obligatory upon himself. It is not subject to legal enforcement, so the judge does not compel him because there is no claimant in this world. For the validity of a vow, it is required that the thing vowed be of a type that is generally obligatory (like fasting, prayer, charity), and it must be an intended act of worship. Thus, acts like wudu and shrouding the dead are excluded. It must not be inherently sinful, and it must not be already obligatory before the vow, and the commitment must not exceed his wealth. So the one who vows is not obliged to do something that is not a category of what is personally obligatory, like visiting the sick, attending a funeral, or entering a mosque. (Fath) 'Absolutely' i.e., without mentioning a condition, such as saying: 'Upon me is to fast for a year' without tying it to anything. ('Ayni) 'Conditional' e.g., 'If Allah heals my sick son, then upon me is such-and-such.' ('Ayni) 'He fulfills it' i.e., he is obliged to fulfill it. He is obligated to fulfill the core act of worship that he committed, not every description he specified; for if he designated a specific dirham, a specific poor person, or a specific place for charity or prayer, that designation is not binding. The obligation is due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever makes a vow and specifies something, he must fulfill what he specified.' This applies if he specified; if he did not specify, then he is required to expiate an oath, whether the vow was absolute or conditional. However, the absolute vow is binding immediately, while the conditional vow becomes binding upon occurrence of the condition. (Fath, Mustakhlas, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi rules regarding vows (nadhr). The matn says: whoever makes a vow, whether absolute (without condition) or conditional, when the condition occurs, must fulfill it. The commentary defines nadhr as a person making an act obligatory upon himself. Such vows are not legally enforceable by a judge because there is no human plaintiff. For a vow to be valid, several conditions are listed: (1) the vowed act must be of a type that is generally considered obligatory (like fasting, prayer, charity); (2) it must be an intended act of worship, excluding acts that are merely means (like wudu) or duties toward others (like shrouding the dead); (3) it must not be inherently sinful; (4) it must not be already obligatory on the person before the vow; (5) the commitment must not exceed one's capacity (e.g., vowing to give away all one's wealth). If these conditions are met, the vow is valid and must be fulfilled. If the vow is absolute, fulfillment is immediate. If conditional, it becomes binding upon the occurrence of the condition. The text also notes a nuance: if the person specifies details like a particular dirham or a specific poor person, those details are not binding; only the core act (e.g., charity) must be done, and he may choose any permissible variation. The proof is the hadith: 'Whoever makes a vow and specifies, he must fulfill what he specified.' If no specification is made (e.g., only 'a vow' without naming), he is required to expiate an oath (kaffara yamin) instead. This is consistent with the principle that undefined vows are treated like oaths requiring expiation.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow: a person making an act of worship obligatory upon himself, either absolutely or conditionally.
مطلق (mutlaq): An absolute vow, not tied to any condition, like saying 'Upon me is to fast for a year'.
معلق بشرط (mu'allaq bi-shart): A conditional vow, e.g., 'If Allah heals my sick son, I must fast'.
تعيين (ta'yin): Specification of details like a particular dirham, person, or place in the vow.
كفارة يمين (kaffara yamin): Expiation for breaking an oath: feeding 10 poor people, clothing them, or freeing a slave; if unable, fasting three days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if someone vows 'I will give one dirham to a specific poor person', but later decides to give to a different person, what is the ruling?
A) He must give to the exact person he named.
B) He may give to anyone in need because the specific designation is not binding.
C) The vow is invalid because it includes a specific detail.
D) He must expiate an oath instead.

Key Takeaway
A valid vow in the Hanafi school requires that the vowed act be a generally obligatory worship; only the core act is binding, while specific details like amount or recipient are flexible.

The Legal Effect of 'Kullu Hallin 'Alayya Haram'
Analysing the statement 'everything permissible is forbidden' and its legal effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two legal consequences of the statement 'kullu hallin 'alayya haram' under Hanafi fiqh and explain why the fatwa differs from the Zahir al-Riwayah position.

Core Idea
The statement triggers either an oath on food/drink or divorce, depending on the level of authority relied upon.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a common but legally complex oath: 'everything permissible is forbidden to me'. We will explore the two main interpretations: the Zahir al-Riwayah view that it applies to food and drink, and the later fatwa that it results in divorce. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a Hanafi jurist dealing with such statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two authority levels
Recognize that the passage presents the ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah (the authoritative transmitted corpus) and the fatwa of the later scholars (mutakhkhirin). They differ in outcome.
2
Analyse the qiyas vs istihsan debate
Understand Zufar's qiyas argument that the word 'kull' implies generality, so any permissible act breaks the oath. Contrast with the majority's istihsan that restricts to food/drink.
This is an example of istihsan overriding qiyas.
3
Determine the effect of intention
If the speaker intends his wife, the statement becomes ila' (vow of abstinence). Without intention, under Zahir al-Riwayah, the wife is not included.
Intention changes the legal characterization.
4
Understand the tarjih to divorce
The later scholars, based on common usage (urf), ruled that the statement results in divorce without need for intention. This is the mufta bihi (operative position).
This tarjih is based on custom, not on the original textual reasoning.
5
Apply to variant formulations
Consider phrases like 'halalu Allahi 'alayya haram' and note the difference of opinion among scholars: some treat it as divorce without intention, others ask the speaker's intention.

كل حل علي :حرام أي إذا قال :الحالف كل حل علي حرام فالمراد به الطعام والشراب فيحنث بالأكل والشرب، وإن قل، إلا أن ينوي غير ذلك، والقياس أن يحنث كما فرغ؛ لأن كلمة "كل" للعموم، وقد باشر فعلاً مباحاً كما فرغ من يمينه، وهو التنفس وفتح العينين ونحوهما. وهو قول زفر محله: وجه الاستحسان أن المقصود يعني البر لا يحصل ذلك مع اعتبار العموم، فيسقط اعتباره، فإذا سقط ينصرف إلى الطعام والشراب المتعارف فإنه يستعمل في ما يتناول عادة، ولا يتناول المرأة إلا بالنية لسقوط اعتبار العموم فإذا نواها كان إيلاء، ولا تصرف اليمين عن المأكول والمشروب لما فيه من التخفيف، حتى إذا أكل أو شرب حنث، كما إذا قرب المرأة، وهذا كله ظاهر الرواية. [رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٨/١] على الطعام والشراب فيحنث بأكله وشربه وإن قل إلا أن ينوي غير ذلك. (ط) والفتوى على أنه إلخ : أي قول الحالف كل" حلال علي "حرام واقع على الطعام والشراب، لكن الفتوى على أنه تبين امرأته بذلك، وهذا قول المتأخرين؛ لأن اللفظ غالب الاستعمال في الطلاق، وقد نقل عن أبي جعفر الله أنه كان متوقفاً في هذه المسألة، وكانت له ابنة صغيرة، فقالت له يوماً يا أبت إن هذا طلاق لها، فقال: كيف عرفت؟ فقالت: لأن هذا مما لا تستعمله النساء، ولو كان غير طلاق لاستعملنها، وإن لم يكن له امرأة يجب عليه الكفارة، واختلف المشايخ في قوله: حلال الله علي حرام أو حلال المسلمين علي حرام، فقيل: ينصرف إلى الطلاق بلا نية للعرف وقيل يسأل عن نيته، فإن نوى يميناً فهي يمين وكفرها، ولا تدخل امرأته في ذلك إلا أن ينويها، فإذا نواها دخلت فيه، فإذا أكل أو شرب أو قرب امرأته حنث وسقط عنه الإيلاء. (مستخلص، عيني) بلا نية: أي نية الطلاق لغلبة استعمال هذا القول في الطلاق ( عيني)
Every permissible thing is forbidden to me: meaning if the one swearing says 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me', then what is intended by it is food and drink, so he breaks his oath by eating and drinking, even if little, unless he intends otherwise. By analogy (qiyas), he would break his oath when he finishes [the oath], because the word 'every' indicates generality, and he has performed a permissible act when he finishes his oath, such as breathing, opening his eyes, and similar. This is the view of Zufar. Its place: the basis of istihsan is that the intended purpose (which is avoidance of the prohibited) is not achieved with consideration of generality, so its consideration drops. When it drops, it turns to the customary food and drink, because it is used in what is customarily consumed, and it does not include the wife except by intention, because consideration of generality drops. If he intends her, it becomes ila' (vow of abstinence). And the oath is not diverted from the eaten and drunk due to what it contains of leniency, so that when he eats or drinks he breaks his oath, just as if he approaches his wife. All this is from Zahir al-Riwayah. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/348) It applies to food and drink, so he breaks his oath by eating and drinking even if little, unless he intends otherwise. (T) And the fatwa is that it leads to divorce without intention. Meaning the statement of the swearer 'every permissible thing is forbidden to me' is applied to food and drink, but the fatwa is that his wife is divorced by that, and this is the statement of the later scholars, because the wording is predominantly used for divorce. It is narrated from Abu Ja'far that he was hesitant about this issue, and he had a young daughter who said to him one day: Father, this divorce [applies] to her. He said: How did you know? She said: Because this is not used by women, and if it were other than divorce, they would use it. If he has no wife, he must make expiation. The scholars differed concerning his statement: 'The permissible of Allah is forbidden to me' or 'The permissible of Muslims is forbidden to me.' Some said: It refers to divorce without intention due to custom. Some said: He is asked about his intention; if he intended an oath, it is an oath and he must expiate it, and his wife does not enter into that unless he intended her. When he intends her, she enters into it, and if he eats, drinks, or approaches his wife, he breaks his oath and the ila' is nullified. (Mustakhlas, Ayni) Without intention: meaning intention of divorce, due to the prevalence of using this statement for divorce (Ayni).
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal effect of the statement 'kullu hallin 'alayya haram' (everything permissible is forbidden to me). It is an expansion on a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The initial ruling is that this statement refers to food and drink, and the oath is broken by eating or drinking, even a small amount, unless the speaker intended otherwise. The passage then contrasts qiyas (analogy) with istihsan. According to qiyas upheld by Zufar, the oath would be broken immediately upon finishing the utterance because the word 'kull' implies generality, and even permissible acts like breathing would break the oath. However, the preferred Hanafi view based on istihsan is that the intended purpose of such oaths (to avoid the halal) is not achieved by adhering to literal generality, so generality is disregarded, and the oath applies specifically to food and drink customarily consumed. The wife is not included unless specifically intended; if intended, it becomes ila' (a vow to abstain from marital relations). This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah. The passage then notes that the fatwa (operative view) of later scholars (mutakhkhirin) is that this statement results in divorce of his wife without any intention, because the common usage of this phrase is for divorce. This is supported by an anecdote about Abu Ja'far. The passage also addresses variant formulations like 'halalu Allahi 'alayya haram' (Allah's permissible is forbidden to me) and notes differences of opinion among scholars.

Key Terms
كل حل علي حرام (kullu hallin 'alayya haram): A statement of oath meaning everything permissible is forbidden to me; in Hanafi fiqh, it primarily applies to food and drink, but by custom and fatwa, it results in divorce.
إيلاء (ila'): A vow to abstain from marital relations; here, if the speaker intends his wife, the declaration becomes ila'.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The transmitted corpus of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani's six books; the ruling here from Zahir al-Riwayah is that the oath applies to food/drink.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It results in divorce of his wife without need for intention.
C) It has no legal effect unless the husband intends divorce.
D) It is treated as a vow of abstinence (ila') in all cases.

Key Takeaway
The statement 'kullu hallin 'alayya haram' has two Hanafi rulings: an oath on food/drink (Zahir al-Riwayah) and divorce (later fatwa); the operative position is divorce based on custom.

Rulings on Vows: Conditions and Fulfillment
Explaining the types of vows and obligations of fulfillment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid vow (nathr), distinguish between unconditional (mutlaq) and conditional (mu'allaq) vows, and apply the ruling that specification is not binding on the details.

Core Idea
A valid vow requires the act to be of a wajib type; unspecified vows require expiation of an oath.

Vows are a form of self-imposed obligation that become binding religiously but not judicially enforceable. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq explains the two types of vows – unconditional and conditional – and the essential conditions that make a vow valid in the Hanafi school. Understanding these conditions is crucial to avoid invalid undertakings or confusion about what must be fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on vows
The passage states that whoever makes a vow, whether unconditional or conditional, must fulfill it (وفى به). This establishes the ruling that fulfillment is wajib.
The ruling is based on a hadith, not on the Quran.
2
Distinguish between unconditional and conditional vows
An unconditional (mutlaq) vow is not attached to any condition (e.g., 'I will fast for a year'), while a conditional (mu'allaq) vow is tied to a condition (e.g., 'If Allah cures my sick, I will fast'). The obligation for unconditional vows is immediate; for conditional vows, it arises when the condition is met.
The passage uses the terms مطلق and معلق.
3
List the conditions for a valid vow
The passage lists five conditions: (1) The vowed act must be of a type that is obligatory (واجب) – e.g., prayer, fasting, charity; (2) It must be an intended act of worship (عبادة مقصودة) – excludes wudu and shrouding; (3) It must not be inherently sinful; (4) It must not be already obligatory before the vow; (5) It must not exceed the person's capacity.
If any condition is missing, the vow is invalid.
4
Understand specification and its effect
If the person specifies details (e.g., a particular poor person, a specific amount), those details are not binding; only the essence of the act is binding. If no specification is made at all (unspecified vow), the person must make expiation of an oath (kafarat yamin) instead of fulfilling the vow.
The hadith 'Whoever vows and specifies must fulfill what he specified' implies that specification narrows the obligation.
5
Recognize the proof from hadith
The primary proof is the hadith: 'Man nadhara wa samma fa'alayhi al-wafa' bima samma' (Whoever vows and specifies must fulfill what he specified). This shows that specification triggers an obligation of fulfillment; without specification, expiation is required.
The imperative 'fa'alayhi' indicates obligation in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[رمز الحقائق: ٣٤٨/١] على الطعام والشراب فيحنث بأكله وشربه وإن قل إلا أن ينوي غير ذلك. (ط) والفتوى على أنه إلخ : أي قول الحالف كل" حلال علي "حرام واقع على الطعام والشراب، لكن الفتوى على أنه تبين امرأته بذلك، وهذا قول المتأخرين؛ لأن اللفظ غالب الاستعمال في الطلاق، وقد نقل عن أبي جعفر الله أنه كان متوقفاً في هذه المسألة، وكانت له ابنة صغيرة، فقالت له يوماً يا أبت إن هذا طلاق لها، فقال: كيف عرفت؟ فقالت: لأن هذا مما لا تستعمله النساء، ولو كان غير طلاق لاستعملنها، وإن لم يكن له امرأة يجب عليه الكفارة، واختلف المشايخ في قوله: حلال الله علي حرام أو حلال المسلمين علي حرام، فقيل: ينصرف إلى الطلاق بلا نية للعرف وقيل يسأل عن نيته، فإن نوى يميناً فهي يمين وكفرها، ولا تدخل امرأته في ذلك إلا أن ينويها، فإذا نواها دخلت فيه، فإذا أكل أو شرب أو قرب امرأته حنث وسقط عنه الإيلاء. (مستخلص، عيني) بلا نية: أي نية الطلاق لغلبة استعمال هذا القول في الطلاق ( عيني) ومن نذر نذراً مطلقاً أو معلقاً بشرط ووجد وفى به . أي نذر نذراً معلقاً أي الشرط أي بالمنذور في الصورتين النذر إيجاب الإنسان على نفسه شيئاً، ولا يدخل تحت الحكم فلا يجبره القاضي؛ لأنه لا مطالب له في الدنيا، ويشترط لصحة النذر أن يكون من جنسه واجب، وأن يكون عبادة مقصودة كصوم وصلاة وصدقة، فخرج الوضوء وتكفين الميت، وأن لا يكون معصية لذاته، وأن لا يكون واجباً عليه قبل النذر، وأن لا يكون ما التزمه أكثر مما يملكه، فلا يلزم الناذر ما ليس من جنسه فرض كعيادة مريض وتشييع جنازة ودخول مسجد . (فتح، مستخلص، عيني) شيئاً،
And whoever makes a vow unconditionally or conditionally, and the condition is met, must fulfill it. That is, he made a conditional vow, meaning the condition, meaning the vowed object in both cases. A vow is the person imposing something on himself, and it does not fall under judicial enforcement, so the judge does not compel him, because there is no claimant in this world. For the validity of a vow, it is required that what is vowed be of a type that is obligatory, that it be an intended act of worship such as fasting, prayer, and charity, thus excluding ablution and shrouding the deceased; that it not be inherently sinful; that it not be already obligatory before the vow; and that what he undertakes not be more than he possesses. Thus the one vowing is not obliged to do what is not of the type of an obligatory act, such as visiting a sick person, attending a funeral procession, or entering a mosque. (Fath) Unconditional: i.e., without mentioning a condition, not restricted by a condition, such as saying 'By Allah, I will fast for a year' without attaching it to anything. (Ayni) Conditional: such as saying, for example, 'If Allah cures my sick, then by Allah I owe such and such.' (Ayni) Fulfill it: i.e., he must fulfill it obligatorily. He is obliged to fulfill the essence of the act of obedience he undertook, not every description he undertook; because if he specified a dirham, a poor person, or a place for charity or prayer, the specification is not binding. The obligation to fulfill is due to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Whoever makes a vow and specifies, he must fulfill what he specified.' This is when he specifies, but if he does not specify, he must make an expiatory oath, whether the vow is unconditional or conditional, but it becomes obligatory immediately for the unconditional and at the time of the condition for the conditional. (Fath, Mustakhlas, Ayni) ... something.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage explains the rulings on vows (nathr). It distinguishes between unconditional (mutlaq) and conditional (mu'allaq) vows. A vow is a person's imposition of an act of worship upon themselves. The judge does not compel fulfillment because there is no human claimant, but it is binding religiously. Conditions for a valid vow: the vowed act must be of a type that is generally obligatory (e.g., prayer, fasting, charity), it must be an intended act of worship (excluding acts like wudu' or shrouding that are means to worship), it must not be sinful, it must not be already obligatory before the vow, and it must not exceed one's capacity. The passage also notes that if the person specifies details (like a particular poor person), the specification is not binding; only the essence of the act is binding. The primary proof is the hadith: 'Whoever vows and specifies must fulfill what he specified.' If no specification is made, expiation of an oath (kafarat yamin) is required. For unconditional vows, the obligation is immediate; for conditional, it arises upon fulfillment of the condition.

Key Terms
نذر (nathr): A vow: an obligation a person imposes on themselves to perform an act of worship.
مطلق (mutlaq): Unconditional: a vow not attached to any condition or event.
معلق (mu'allaq): Conditional: a vow attached to a condition, e.g., 'If Allah cures my sick, I will fast.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A valid vow must be for an act that is wajib in type, and while the essence must be fulfilled, specifications are not binding; unspecified vows require expiation.
